Resolving Ambiguity in Definitions with Qarina
Author defends definition against ambiguity objection using qarina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether an ambiguous term invalidates a definition by identifying the qarina that specifies the intended meaning.

Core Idea
A qarina turns an ambiguous term into a precise definitional tool.

In Islamic logic, definitions must be precise. When an objector claims that the term 'ilm (knowledge) is ambiguous – meaning both mental acquisition and firm belief – the definition of mantiq appears flawed. The author responds by introducing the concept of qarina: a contextual indicator that pins down the intended meaning. This lesson unpacks that defense and shows how qarina functions in definitions across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the objection in the Arabic: 'It should not be said: 'ilm is among the ambiguous terms...' The objector claims that 'ilm has multiple meanings, so using it in a definition violates the rule against ambiguity.
2
Understand the ambiguity of 'ilm
The two meanings are: (1) mental acquisition (al-husul al-'aqli), a broad cognitive state; (2) firm, conforming, constant belief (al-i'tiqad al-jazim al-mutabiq al-thabit), which is more specific. The objection says this ambiguity makes the definition invalid.
3
Locate the qarina
The author argues that the qarina is the fact that the matn author only explains 'ilm as mental acquisition in this book. This contextual evidence specifies which meaning is intended.
Qarina can be textual or contextual; here it is the author's own usage in the same work.
4
See how the qarina resolves ambiguity
With the qarina, the term 'ilm in the definition refers unequivocally to mental acquisition. Therefore, the definition does not violate the condition of avoiding ambiguity, because ambiguity is removed by the indicator.
5
Extract the general principle
The author establishes that ambiguous terms are permissible in definitions if a clear qarina specifies the intended meaning. This is a standard principle in usul al-fiqh and mantiq.
This principle applies beyond this example, e.g., in legal definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكقولنا : «هذا الحائط ينتثر منه التراب، وكل حائط ينتثر منه التراب ينهدم، فهذا الحائط ينهدم وأما في الجهلي فكما إذا قيل : «العالم مستغن عن المؤثر، وكل مستغن عن المؤثر قديم، فالعالم قديم».
لا يقال : العلم من الألفاظ المشتركة، فإنّه كما يطلق على الحصول العقلي، كذلك يطلق على الاعتقاد الجازم المطابق الثابت، وهو أخص من الأول؛ ومن شرائط التعريفات التحرز عن استعمال الألفاظ المشتركة.
لانا نقول : الألفاظ المشتركة لا تستعمل في التعريفات إلا إذا قامت قرينة تدلّ على تعيين المراد من معانيها، وهاهنا قرينة دالة على أن المراد بالعلم المذكور في التعريف الحصول العقلي، فإنّه لم يفسره في هذا الكتاب إلا به. وإنما اعتبر «الجهل» في المطلوب " حيث قال : «للتأدي إلى المجهول» لاستحالة استعلام المعلوم وتحصيل الحاصل. وهو أعم من أن يكون تصورياً أو تصديقياً. أما المجهول التصوّري فاكتسابه من الأمور التصورية؛ وأما المجهول التصديقي فاكتسابه من الأمور التصديقية
(۱) مبادئ المطلوب لابد أن تكون معلومة – أي حاصلة قبل حصوله - ليتصور التركيب فيها ؛ فلذلك قال : ترتيب أمور معلومة». وأما المطلوب فينبغي لا يكون معلوماً وحاصلا من الوجه الذي يطلب من النظر تحصيله وإن وجب
أن يكون معلوماً بوجه آخر حتى يمكن طلبه بالاختيار (شريف).
أن
(٢) يعني أن طريق اكتساب التصوّر من التصورات وطريق اكتساب التصديق من التصديقات معلومان، وأما طريق اكتساب التصور من التصديقات أو بالعكس
فمما لم يتحقق وجوده، وإن لم يقم برهان أيضاً على امتناعه (شريف).
So our statement: 'This wall, dust falls from it, and every wall from which dust falls collapses, so this wall collapses.' As for the ignorant, it is like when it is said: 'The world is independent of an effect, and everything independent of an effect is eternal, so the world is eternal.' It should not be said: 'ilm is among the ambiguous terms, for just as it is applied to mental acquisition, it is also applied to the firm, conforming, constant belief, and it is more specific than the first; and among the conditions of definitions is to avoid using ambiguous terms.' For we say: Ambiguous terms are not used in definitions unless there is a qarina (contextual indicator) that indicates the intended meaning from among its meanings, and here there is a qarina indicating that what is meant by 'ilm mentioned in the definition is mental acquisition, for he [the author] did not explain it in this book except with that. He only considered 'jahl' in the desired [conclusion], where he said: 'to lead to the unknown' because it is impossible to learn the known and to obtain that which is already obtained. It is more general than being tasawwuri or tasdiqi. As for the unknown tasawwuri, its acquisition is from tasawwuri matters; as for the unknown tasdiqi, its acquisition is from tasdiqi matters. (1) The principles of the desired [conclusion] must be known – i.e., obtained before its obtaining – in order to conceive the composition in them; therefore he said: 'arrangement of known things.' As for the desired [conclusion], it should not be known and obtained from the aspect that is sought through investigation to obtain it, although it must be known in another aspect so that it can be sought by choice (Sharif). That (2) means that the way of acquiring tasawwur from tasawwurat and the way of acquiring tasdiq from tasdiqat are known, but as for the way of acquiring tasawwur from tasdiqat or vice versa, it is something whose existence has not been verified, although no proof has been established for its impossibility either (Sharif).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) addressing an objection to a definition of logic (mantiq). The author first provides examples of argument forms: one from physical causation (wall crumbling) and another from philosophical reasoning (world independent of effect). Then an objection is raised: the term 'ilm (knowledge) used in the definition is ambiguous (mushtarak) because it can mean either mental acquisition (husul 'aqli) or firm belief (i'tiqad jazim). Ambiguity is normally avoided in definitions. The response is that ambiguous terms are permissible when a qarina (contextual indicator) specifies the intended meaning. Here, the qarina is that the author of the matn (text) only explains 'ilm as mental acquisition in this book. Furthermore, the definition uses 'jahl (ignorance)' to lead to the unknown, implying that the unknown includes both conceptual (tasawwuri) and assertive (tasdiqi) unknowns. The marginal note by Sharif further explains that the principles of the desired conclusion must be known before the investigation, and that the way to acquire conceptual knowledge is from conceptual matters, and assertive knowledge from assertive matters, while the cross-acquisition is not verified.

العلم
الجهل
القرينة

C) Because ambiguous terms are always allowed in definitions.
D) Because 'ilm has only one meaning in logic.

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous term in a definition is acceptable if a clear qarina (contextual indicator) specifies which meaning is intended, as demonstrated by the defense of 'ilm in the definition of mantiq.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
5 min
The Four Causes Embedded in the Definition of Nazar
Author explains how the definition of nazar contains the four causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how each of the four Aristotelian causes is embedded in the definition of nazar (reasoning).

Core Idea
A complete definition includes all four causes—formal, material, efficient, final.

The definition of nazar as 'arrangement of known matters leading to the unknown' is more than a mere description; it deliberately contains the four causes. Understanding this reveals the depth of Islamic logical definitions and sets a standard for completeness.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of nazar
The definition is 'al-tartīb al-umūr al-ma‘lūma li al-ta’dī ilā majhūl' (arrangement of known matters leading to the unknown). This is the central definiendum.
2
Identify the formal cause
The term 'tartīb' (arrangement) itself indicates the formal cause: the structured configuration (hay’a ijtimā‘iyya) of concepts and assents.
The author claims this is by direct correspondence (muṭābaqa).
3
Identify the efficient cause
Every arrangement requires an arranger; thus 'tartīb' implies an efficient cause (the reasoning person, al-nāẓir) by implication (iltizām).
4
Identify the material cause
The 'known matters' (al-umūr al-ma‘lūma) serve as the material cause – the content that is arranged. The text notes this is metaphorical since nazar is a psychic accident.
Matter and form normally belong to bodies.
5
Identify the final cause
The phrase 'leading to an unknown' (al-ta’dī ilā majhūl) indicates the final cause: the purpose of reasoning is to derive new knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن لطائف هذا التعريف أنه مشتمل على العلل الأربع فـ«الترتيب» إشارة إلى العلة الصورية بالمطابقة، فإن صورة الفكر هي الهيأة الاجتماعية الحاصلة للتصورات والتصديقات، كالهيأة الحاصلة لأجزاء السرير في اجتماعها وترتيبها. وإلى العلة الفاعلية بالالتزام، إذ لا بد لكلِّ ترتيب من ،مرتب، وهي القوة الفاعلة، كالنجار للسرير. (1) كل مركب صادر عن فاعل مختار لابد له من علّة مادية وعلة صورية - وهما داخلتان فيه - ومن علّة فاعليّة وعلة غائيّة، وهما خارجتان عنه؛ وقديعرف الشيء بالقياس إلى علة واحدة أو علتين أو ثلاث، وإذا عرف بالأربع كان ذلك أكمل من باقي الأقسام ؛ وليس المراد من التعريف بالعلل أن تكون هي بنفسها معرفة - لأنها مباينة للمعلول - بل المراد أنه يؤخذ للمعلول بالقياس إلى العلل محمولات عليه فيعرف بها. وماذكره من أن «فاعل النظر هو المرتب الناظر، وأن غايته هو التأدي إلى مجهول» فهو قول تحقيقي، وأمّا أنّ الأمور المعلومة مادية، وأن الهيأة العارضة لتلك الأمور صورية فهو قول على سبيل التشبيه، لأن النظر من الأعراض النفسانية والمادة والصورة إنما تكونان للأجسام (شريف). (2) اعترض عليه بأن صورة الفكر - كما اعترف به - هي الهيأة الاجتماعية، ولاشك أنها ليست نفس الترتيب، بل هي معلولة له فيكون دلالة الترتيب عليها التزامية كدلالته على المرتب. ويمكن أن يقال : إن دلالة الترتيب على الهيأة - التي هي المعلولة له - أظهر من دلالته على المرتب - الذي هو فاعله - لأن دلالة العلة على معلولها أقوى وأظهر من دلالة المعلول على علته، لأن العلة المعيّنة تدل على معلول معين والمعلول المعين يدلّ على علة ما، فأراد التنبيه على ذلك فعبر بالمطابقة على معنى أن دلالة الترتيب على الهيأة كالمطابقة في الظهور (شريف).
Among the subtleties of this definition is that it comprises the four causes. For 'arrangement' (al-tartib) indicates the formal cause (al-‘illa al-ṣūriyya) by direct correspondence (bi-l-muṭābaqa), for the form of thought is the social configuration (al-hay’a al-ijtimā‘iyya) acquired by the concepts (taṣawwurāt) and assents (taṣdīqāt), like the configuration acquired by the parts of a bed in their gathering and arrangement. And it indicates the efficient cause (al-‘illa al-fā‘iliyya) by implication (bi-l-iltizām), since every arrangement must have an arranger, which is the active force, like the carpenter for the bed. (1) Every composite issuing from a free agent must have a material cause and a formal cause – both are internal to it – and an efficient cause and a final cause, both external to it. A thing may be defined in relation to one cause or two or three, and if defined by all four, that is more complete than the other divisions. The intention of definition by causes is not that the causes themselves are the definiens – for they are distinct from the effect – but rather that the effect is taken in relation to the causes as predicates upon it and thus known. And what he mentioned, that 'the agent of reasoning (nazar) is the reasoning arranger, and its final cause is leading to an unknown,' is a truthful statement. As for the claim that the known matters are material, and the configuration that accrues to those matters is formal, that is a metaphorical statement, because reasoning (nazar) is among the psychic accidents, whereas matter and form belong only to bodies (Sharif). (2) It was objected to him that the form of thought – as he admitted – is the social configuration, and there is no doubt that it is not itself the arrangement, but rather it is the effect of it. So the indication of arrangement upon it is implicative, like its indication upon the arranger. And it can be said: The indication of arrangement upon the configuration – which is its effect – is more apparent than its indication upon the arranger – which is its agent – because the indication of the cause upon its effect is stronger and more apparent than the indication of the effect upon its cause, since a determinate cause indicates a determinate effect, while a determinate effect indicates some cause. So he wanted to alert to that, and thus he expressed it as 'direct correspondence' (muṭābaqa) meaning that the indication of arrangement upon the configuration is like direct correspondence in clarity (Sharif).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage elaborates on a subtlety in the definition of nazar (reasoning) as 'arrangement of known things leading to the unknown'. The commentator explains that the definition contains all four causes: (1) The formal cause is indicated by 'arrangement' (al-tartib) directly, because the form of thought is the composition of concepts and assents, like the structure of a bed. (2) The efficient cause is implied by the need for an arranger, i.e., the reasoning agent. A marginal note by Sharif clarifies that definition by all four causes is the most complete, though it must be understood that the causes are not the definiens itself but predicates used to define the effect. Then an objection is raised: the form of thought (social configuration) is the effect of arrangement, not the arrangement itself, so indication should be implicative, not direct. The response is that the indication of cause (arrangement) on effect (configuration) is so clear that it is like direct correspondence. This demonstrates the subtlety in claiming that 'arrangement' directly indicates the formal cause, because the relation is causal and thus very apparent.

Key Terms
العلة الصورية
العلة الفاعلية
العلة المادية
العلة الغائية
المطابقة
الالتزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author say that 'arrangement' indicates the formal cause by direct correspondence (muṭābaqa)?
A) Because arrangement and form are identical.
B) Because the indication of a cause on its effect is so clear it appears like direct correspondence.
C) Because the formal cause is the arrangement of the bed.
D) Because the arrangement implies an arranger.

Key Takeaway
The definition of nazar contains all four causes, demonstrating the completeness and subtlety of Islamic logical definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyse the definition of 'salat' in fiqh: 'salat is specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim with intention.' Identify which of the four causes are present in this definition and which are missing. Explain how a complete definition would include all four.

What Is Mantiq?
advanced
5 min
Distinguishing Muṭābaqa and Iltizām in the Definition of Nazar
Author and commentator discuss whether 'arrangement' indicates the formal cause by muṭābaqa or iltizām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct correspondence (muṭābaqa) and implication (iltizām) in the context of the definition of nazar, and explain how causal relations can affect the type of indication.

Core Idea
The same term can indicate different things by muṭābaqa or iltizām, and causal relations can make an implication appear direct.

The author claimed that 'arrangement' directly indicates the formal cause. An objector pointed out that since the formal cause is the effect of arrangement, the indication should be implicative. The response reveals a subtlety about the strength of causal indication: a cause indicates its effect so clearly that it functions like direct correspondence.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three types of indication
In Islamic logic, terms indicate meanings by muṭābaqa (direct correspondence), tazamun (concomitance), or iltizām (implication). Muṭābaqa is when the term refers to its intended meaning without intermediary.
2
Identify the formal cause as the effect of arrangement
The formal cause is the configuration of thoughts (hay’a ijtimā‘iyya), which is produced by the arrangement. Thus arrangement is the cause, configuration is the effect.
3
Understand the objection
Since configuration is not the same as arrangement, the indication of 'arrangement' upon it is technically iltizām (implication), not muṭābaqa, because the meaning is not directly given but inferred.
The objector argues: arrangement → configuration is a causal relation, so it is implicative.
4
Analyze the response about causal indication
The response states that the indication of a cause on its effect is stronger and more apparent than the indication of an effect on its cause. A determinate cause points to a determinate effect, so it is treated as muṭābaqa in clarity.
Thus the author used muṭābaqa metaphorically to highlight the clarity of the causal link.
5
Apply the distinction to Islamic sciences
In definitions like that of 'iman' or 'salat', recognizing whether a part of the definition is indicated by muṭābaqa or iltizām affects how we understand the essence and implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(1) كل مركب صادر عن فاعل مختار لابد له من علّة مادية وعلة صورية - وهما داخلتان فيه - ومن علّة فاعليّة وعلة غائيّة، وهما خارجتان عنه؛ وقديعرف الشيء بالقياس إلى علة واحدة أو علتين أو ثلاث، وإذا عرف بالأربع كان ذلك أكمل من باقي الأقسام ؛ وليس المراد من التعريف بالعلل أن تكون هي بنفسها معرفة - لأنها مباينة للمعلول - بل المراد أنه يؤخذ للمعلول بالقياس إلى العلل محمولات عليه فيعرف بها. وماذكره من أن «فاعل النظر هو المرتب الناظر، وأن غايته هو التأدي إلى مجهول» فهو قول تحقيقي، وأمّا أنّ الأمور المعلومة مادية، وأن الهيأة العارضة لتلك الأمور صورية فهو قول على سبيل التشبيه، لأن النظر من الأعراض النفسانية والمادة والصورة إنما تكونان للأجسام (شريف). How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the definition of 'iman' as 'belief in the heart, utterance on the tongue, and action with the limbs,' identify which parts of the definition are indicated by muṭābaqa (direct correspondence) and which by iltizām (implication). For example, does 'belief in the heart' directly indicate the essence of iman, or does it imply something else?

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
10 min
The Need for Logic and Its Naming
Author explains the need for logic due to error in human thought and defines it as the law for correct reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is necessary for correct reasoning and identify the derivation of its name from the rational faculty.

Core Idea
Logic is the law that prevents error in thought and manifests the rational faculty.

This passage from a commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya establishes that human thought is prone to error—even sincere thinkers contradict each other and themselves. To avoid this, we need a law that distinguishes correct from corrupt reasoning. That law is logic, and its name derives from the rational faculty (nutq) it perfects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: human thought is fallible
The author argues that not all thought is correct because rational people contradict each other (e.g., one believes the world is created, another that it is eternal), and even a single person contradicts himself over time. This shows error occurs despite seeking truth.
The example from kalam (creation vs. eternity) is used to illustrate real contradictions.
2
Extract the function of logic
Logic is defined as a law (qanun) that provides knowledge of the methods for acquiring theoretical matters (nazariyyat) and distinguishes correct from corrupt thoughts. It is the tool that ensures sound reasoning.
The law is necessary because the natural intellect alone does not suffice.
3
Analyze the derivation of the name 'mantiq'
The name 'mantiq' comes from 'nutq' (rational faculty). Logic brings about the manifestation (zuhur) of this faculty, perfecting it and directing it to correctness.
'Nutq' includes both external speech and internal intellection.
4
Understand the scope: concepts and assents
The marginal note by Sharif explains that logic covers both conceptual (tasawwur) and assensional (tasdiq) knowledge. For any sought matter, one must identify the correct principles (material) and the proper method (form) to avoid error.
This shows logic is not just about arguments but about all knowledge acquisition.
5
Connect to Islamic sciences
Logic is the guarantor of correct reasoning in disciplines like kalam and fiqh. Without it, one cannot reliably distinguish valid from invalid arguments about foundational beliefs.
The passage itself uses a kalam example (huduth al-alam) to motivate the need for logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح المقدمة
٥٧
و «أمور معلومة» إشارة إلى العلة المادية، كقطع الخشب للسرير. و« للتأدي إلى مجهول» إشارة إلى العلة الغائية، فإن الغرض من ذلك الترتيب ليس إلا أن يتأدّى الذهن إلى المطلوب المجهول، كجلوس السلطان - مثلاً - للسرير.
وذلك الترتيب - أي الفكر - ليس بصواب دائماً، لأن بعض العقلاء يناقض بعضاً في مقتضى أفكارهم ، فمن واحد يتأدى فكره إلى التصديق بحدوث العالم، ومن آخر إلى التصديق بقدمه ؛ بل الإنسان الواحد يناقض نفسه بحسب الوقتين، فقد يفكّر ويؤدّي فكره إلى التصديق بقدم العالم، ثم يفكر وينساق فكره إلى التصديق بحدوثه؛ فالفكران ليسا بصوابين، وإلا لزم اجتماع النقيضين، فلايكون كل فكر صواباً.
فمست الحاجة إلى قانون يفيد معرفة طرق اكتساب النظريات
(۱) دلّ هذا على أن الفكر قديكون خطأ، وأن بداهة العقل لاتفي بتمييز الخطاء عن الصواب، والألماوقع الخطأ من العقلاء الطالبين للصواب، الهاربين عن الخطأ. وإنما قال : «بل الإنسان الواحد يناقض نفسه في وقتين» لأنه أظهر، فإنّ العاقل المفكر إذا فتش عن أحواله وجد أنه يعتقد أموراً متناقضة بحسب أوقات مختلفة. أي يفكر في وقت ويعتقد حكماً، ثم يفكر في وقت آخر ويعتقد حكماً آخر مناقضاً للحكم الأول؛ فالوقتان إنماهما للفكرين وأما النتيجتان فمشتملتان على اتحاد الزمان المعتبر في التناقض، واقتصر على بيان الخطاء في الأفكار الكاسبة للتصديقات، لعدم ظهور ذلك في التصورات (شريف). ) يريد أن المقصود وإن كان معرفة تفاصيل أحوال الأنظار الجزئية، لكنها متعذرة؛ فلابد من قانون يُرجع إليه في معرفة أحوال أي نظر أريد من الأنظار المخصوصة
(شریف).
٥٨
شرح الرسالة الشمسية
التصورية والتصديقية من ضرورياتهما ، و الإحاطة بالأفكار الصحيحة والفاسدة الواقعة فيها - أي في تلك الطرق - حتّى يُعرف منه أن كل
۲)
نظر بأي طريق يكتسب ؟ وأي فكر صحيح ؟ وأي فكر فاسد ) ؟ وذلك القانون هو المنطق.
وإنما سمي به ، لأن ظهور القوة النطقية إنما يحصل بسببه
d)
(١) لم يرد أن اكتساب النظريات إنما يكون من الضروريات ابتداء بل أراد أن اكتسابها إنما يستند إلى الضروريات إما ابتداء أو بواسطة، الجواز أن يكتسب نظري من نظري آخر، ويكتسب ذلك النظري الآخر من نظري ثالث - وهكذا - لكن لابد من الانتهاء إلى الضروريات دفعاً للدور أو التسلسل (شريف). (۲) قد عرفت أن للفكر مادة هي الأمور المعلومة، وصورة هي الهيأة الاجتماعية اللازمة للترتيب، فإذا صحنا كان الفكر صحيحاً، أو فسدتا معاً أو فسدت إحداهما كان فاسداً، فإذا أريد اكتساب تصوّر لم يمكن ذلك من أي تصور كان، بل لابد له من تصورات لها مناسبة مخصوصة إلى ذلك التصور المطلوب، وكذا الحال في التصديقات؛ فلكل مطلوب من المطالب التصورية والتصديقية مباد معينة يكتسب منها. ثم إن اكتسابه من تلك المبادئ لا يمكن أن يكون بأي طريق كان، بل لابد هناك من طريق مخصوص له شرائط مخصوصة، فيحتاج في كل مطلوب إلى شيئين : أحدهما تميز مبادئه عن غيرها، والثاني معرفة الطريق المخصوص الواقع في تلك المبادئ مع شرائطه ؛ فإذا حصل مبادؤه وسلك فيها ذلك الطريق، أصيب إلى المطلوب؛ فإن وقع خطأ - إما في المبادئ أو في الطريق - لم يصب، والمتكفّل بتحصيل هذين الأمرين كما ينبغي هو هذا الفن (شريف). (٣) النطق يطلق على النطق الظاهري - وهو التكلّم - وعلى النطق الباطني - وهو
إدراك المعقولات - وهذا الفنّ يقوي الأول ويسلك بالثاني مسلك السداد
Explanation of the Introduction 57. And 'known things' is a reference to the material cause, like cutting wood for a bed. And 'to lead to the unknown' is a reference to the final cause, for the purpose of that arrangement is only that the mind arrives at the unknown sought, like the sitting of the sultan - for example - on the bed. And that arrangement - meaning thought - is not always correct, because some rational people contradict others regarding the implications of their thoughts. For one person's thought leads to belief in the world's temporal origination, and another's to belief in its pre-eternity. Rather, a single person contradicts himself according to two times. For he may think and his thought leads him to belief in the world's pre-eternity, then he thinks and his thought proceeds to belief in its temporal origination. So the two thoughts are not both correct, otherwise the conjunction of two contradictories would be necessary. Therefore not every thought is correct. So there is a need for a law that gives knowledge of the methods of acquiring theoretical matters. (1) This indicates that thought may be erroneous and that the natural intellect does not suffice to distinguish error from truth, and that error occurs among rational people who seek truth and flee from error. And he only said 'rather, the single person contradicts himself in two times' because it is more apparent. For the rational thoughtful person, if he examines his states, finds that he believes contradictory things according to different times. That is, he thinks at one time and believes a judgment, then thinks at another time and believes another judgment contradicting the first. So the two times are only for the two thoughts, while the two results include the unity of time considered in contradiction. And he confined himself to showing error in thoughts that acquire assents, because that is not apparent in concepts (Sharif). He intends that although the purpose is to know the details of particular considerations, yet they are impossible; so there must be a law to which one refers in knowing the states of any consideration intended from particular considerations (Sharif). 58. Explanation of al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. Conceptual and assensional [matters] from their necessary [premises], and encompassing the correct and corrupt thoughts occurring in them - that is, in those methods - so that it is known therefrom: every consideration, by which method is it acquired? And which thought is correct? And which thought is corrupt? And that law is logic. And it is only called by this name because the manifestation of the rational faculty only occurs by means of it. (1) He did not intend that the acquisition of theoretical matters is only from necessary [premises] at the outset, but he intended that their acquisition is based on necessary [premises] either directly or by mediation, because it is possible that a theoretical [matter] be acquired from another theoretical [matter], and that other theoretical [matter] be acquired from a third theoretical [matter], and so on, but it is necessary to end at necessary [premises] to avoid circularity or infinite regress (Sharif). (2) You have known that thought has a material [cause] which is the known things, and a form which is the social configuration necessary for arrangement. So if both are sound, the thought is correct; if both are corrupt or one of them is corrupt, it is corrupt. So if one wants to acquire a concept, it cannot be from any concept whatever, but it must have concepts that have a specific suitability to that sought concept. And similarly for assents; for every sought [matter] from conceptual and assensional demands, there are certain principles from which it is acquired. Then its acquisition from those principles cannot be by any method whatever, but there must be a specific method with specific conditions. So in every sought [matter], two things are needed: first, distinguishing its principles from others; second, knowing the specific method applied to those principles with its conditions. So if its principles are obtained and that method is followed in them, the sought [matter] is attained. If an error occurs - either in the principles or in the method - it is not attained. And the guarantor of obtaining these two things as they should be is this discipline (Sharif). (3) 'Natiq' is applied to external speech - which is speech - and to internal speech - which is the perception of intelligibles. And this discipline strengthens the first and directs the second to the path of correctness.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This is a passage from a commentary (sharh) on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. The author is explaining the definition and necessity of logic. He begins by unpacking two key phrases: 'known things' (amoor ma'luma) refers to the material cause of thought—like cutting wood for a bed—and 'to lead to the unknown' refers to the final cause—the purpose of thinking is to arrive at a previously unknown conclusion. The author then argues that not every thought (fikr) is correct. He gives examples: different rational people may arrive at contradictory conclusions (e.g., one believes the world is created, another that it is eternal), and even a single person may contradict himself at different times. If both thoughts were true, contradictions would coexist, which is impossible. Therefore, we need a law (qanun) to know how to acquire theoretical knowledge correctly. The marginal note by Sharif adds that thought can be erroneous even among sincere truth-seekers, and that the example of self-contradiction is more striking. The author then states that this law is logic (mantiq). The name 'mantiq' derives from 'nutq' (rational faculty), because logic perfects and manifests that faculty. Sharif's notes further elaborate: logic covers both the material (premises) and formal (arrangement) aspects of reasoning, and it is necessary to ensure that both the principles and the method are correct. He also explains that in acquiring any sought knowledge, one must identify the appropriate principles and the specific method, and this is the function of logic. Finally, 'nutq' is explained as both external speech and internal intellection; logic strengthens both, directing internal reasoning correctly and articulating it externally.

Key Terms
المنطق
الفكر
القوة النطقية
النظريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the discipline called 'mantiq' (logic)?
A) Because it is a spoken art.
B) Because it brings about the manifestation of the rational faculty.
C) Because it deals with arguments.
D) Because it is a tool for theology.

Key Takeaway
Human thought is fallible, so a law (logic) is needed to guide reasoning to truth and to perfect the rational faculty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated. Everything temporally originated has a cause. Therefore, the world has a cause.' Identify the 'known things' (material cause) and the 'arrangement' (form) in this argument, as described in the passage.

Explain in your own words why the passage argues that a single person can contradict himself over time, and how logic serves as a remedy for such contradictions.

What Is Mantiq?

5 min
Logic as a Law (Qanun) Protecting the Mind
Author explains that logic is a law distinguishing correct from incorrect thought and derives its name from nutq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of logic as a law (qanun) and the etymology of its name from nutq.

Core Idea
Logic is the law that safeguards the rational faculty from error in both thought and speech.

This lesson introduces the classical definition of logic as a regulatory law (qanun) for the mind. The passage explains why logic is called mantiq—derived from nutq, the rational faculty that manifests in both internal intellection and external speech. It also establishes the need for logic to prevent circular reasoning and infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term 'mantiq' in the Arabic and note that it is defined as a 'qanun' (law). The author states: 'وذلك القانون هو المنطق.'
The term 'qanun' here means a set of rules governing correct thinking.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'law' (qanun). The differentia is 'that distinguishes correct from incorrect thoughts' (الإحاطة بالأفكار الصحيحة والفاسدة).
3
Analyze the derivation of the name
The name 'mantiq' is derived from 'nutq' (نطق), which refers to both external speech (التكلّم) and internal intellection (إدراك المعقولات). Logic strengthens both aspects.
This shows that logic is not merely about language but about the rational faculty itself.
4
Understand the need for grounding in necessary knowledge
The footnote clarifies that theoretical knowledge must ultimately trace back to necessary knowledge (ضروريات) to avoid circularity or infinite regress.
This establishes the epistemological foundation of logic.

Source Text

(2) قد عرفت أن للفكر مادة هي الأمور المعلومة، وصورة هي الهيأة الاجتماعية اللازمة للترتيب، فإذا صحنا كان الفكر صحيحاً، أو فسدتا معاً أو فسدت إحداهما كان فاسداً، فإذا أريد اكتساب تصوّر لم يمكن ذلك من أي تصور كان، بل لابد له من تصورات لها مناسبة مخصوصة إلى ذلك التصور المطلوب، وكذا الحال في التصديقات؛ فلكل مطلوب من المطالب التصورية والتصديقية مباد معينة يكتسب منها. ثم إن اكتسابه من تلك المبادئ لا يمكن أن يكون بأي طريق كان، بل لابد هناك من طريق مخصوص له شرائط مخصوصة، فيحتاج في كل مطلوب إلى شيئين : أحدهما تميز مبادئه عن غيرها، والثاني معرفة الطريق المخصوص الواقع في تلك المبادئ مع شرائطه ؛ فإذا حصل مبادؤه وسلك فيها ذلك الطريق، أصيب إلى المطلوب؛ فإن وقع خطأ - إما في المبادئ أو في الطريق - لم يصب، والمتكفّل بتحصيل هذين الأمرين كما ينبغي هو هذا الفن (شريف). (3) النطق يطلق على النطق الظاهري - وهو التكلّم - وعلى النطق الباطني - وهو إدراك المعقولات - وهذا الفنّ يقوي الأول ويسلك بالثاني مسلك السداد. فبهذا الفنّ يتقوى ويظهر كلا معنيي النطق للنفس الإنسانية المسماة بالناطقة، فاشتق له اسم من النطق (شريف).
Concepts and assents are among necessary things, and encompassing correct and incorrect thoughts occurring in them - i.e., in those paths - so that it is known from it how every consideration (nazar) is acquired by which path, and which thought is correct and which is faulty. And that law is logic. It is only named by it because the manifestation of the rational faculty (quwwa natiqa) is attained by means of it. (1) He did not intend that the acquisition of theoretical matters is only from necessary matters initially, but rather he intended that their acquisition is based on necessary matters either initially or through an intermediary. It is permissible that a theoretical matter be acquired from another theoretical matter, and that other theoretical matter be acquired from a third theoretical matter - and so on - but it must end at necessary matters to avoid circularity or infinite regress. (2) You have known that thought has a material which is known things, and a form which is the relational structure necessary for order. So if both are sound, the thought is correct; if both are corrupt or one of them is corrupt, it is faulty. So if one wants to acquire a concept, it cannot be from any concept whatsoever, but it must have concepts with a specific suitability to that desired concept. Similarly for assents. So for each sought matter among conceptual and assential demands, there are specific principles from which it is acquired. Then its acquisition from those principles cannot be by any path, but there must be a specific path with specific conditions. So for every desired matter, two things are needed: first, distinguishing its principles from others; second, knowing the specific path that occurs in those principles along with its conditions. So if its principles are obtained and that path is followed in them, one arrives at the desired matter; if a mistake occurs - either in the principles or in the path - one does not arrive. And that which undertakes to obtain these two things as they ought is this art. (3) Nutq is applied to external nutq - which is speech - and to internal nutq - which is intellection of intelligibles. And this art strengthens the first and makes the second follow the path of correctness. So by this art, both meanings of nutq are strengthened and manifested for the human soul called natiqa, so it is derived from nutq.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines logic as a law (qanun) that safeguards the mind from error in thinking. The author begins by stating that concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat) are necessary starting points, and that logic encompasses the correct and incorrect thoughts that occur within these categories. The purpose is to distinguish how each inquiry (nazar) is acquired and which thoughts are correct or faulty. This law is logic. The name 'mantiq' is derived from 'nutq', which refers to the rational faculty manifesting in both external speech and internal intellection. The footnotes further clarify: theoretical knowledge must ultimately trace back to necessary knowledge to avoid circularity or infinite regress; thought consists of matter (known things) and form (order), and for each desired concept or judgment there are specific principles and a specific path; and the art of logic strengthens both the external and internal aspects of rational speech.

Logic as a Tool: Definition and Causal Distinction
Author defines logic and explains 'ālah' with objection-answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of logic as a canonical tool and distinguish between a tool (ālah) and an intermediate cause (illa mutawassiṭa) using the causal chain example.

Core Idea
Logic is a tool that transmits the agent's effect directly, unlike an intermediate cause.

This lesson unpacks the formal definition of logic given by the logicians: 'a canonical tool whose observance protects the mind from error in thought.' The commentary focuses on the word 'tool' (ālah) and clarifies it by contrasting it with an intermediate cause, using a causal chain example. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how logic functions as a safeguard for the mind.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Identify the three components of the definition: ālah (tool), qānūniyya (canonical), and the purpose (protection from error). These are the elements you will analyse.
The definition is given at the beginning of the passage.
2
Define 'ālah' as given
The commentary defines 'ālah' as an intermediary between an agent and its patient in delivering the effect. Note the example of the saw for the carpenter.
This is the core meaning used in the definition.
3
Understand the exclusionary clause
The phrase 'in the delivery of its effect to it' is added to exclude intermediate causes (illa mutawassiṭa). Why? Because intermediate causes do not directly transmit the effect; they mediate causation without transmission.
This is the key nuance of the definition.
4
Work through the objection and answer
The objection argues that if the remote cause's effect does not reach the patient, then the intermediate cause should not be considered an intermediary, making the last restriction unnecessary. The answer counters that the remote cause still has a role, so the intermediate cause is an intermediary in a general sense, necessitating the restriction.
The example uses A, B, C to illustrate the causal chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة. ورسموه بأنه : آلة قانونية تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطا في الفكر». فالآلة هي الواسطة بين الفاعل و منفعله في وصول أثره إليه، كالمنشار للنجار، فإنه واسطة بينه وبين الخشب في وصول أثره إليه، فالقيد الأخير لإخراج العلّة المتوسطة فإنها واسطة بين فاعلها ومنفعلها إذ علة علة الشيء، علة لذلك الشيء بالواسطة، فإن (أ) إذا كان علة لـ(ب) و (ب) علة ل(ج) كان (أ) علّة لـ(ج) ولكن بواسطة (ب) إلا أنها ليست بواسطة بينهما في وصول أثر العلة البعيدة إلى المعلول، لأن أثر العلة البعيدة لا يصل إلى المعلول، فضلاً عن أن يتوسط في ذلك شيء آخر، و فبهذا الفنّ يتقوى ويظهر كلا معنيي النطق للنفس الإنسانية المسماة بالناطقة، فاشتق له اسم من النطق (شريف). (۱) قيل عليه : فعلى هذا لا يكون المعلول منفعلاً عن العلة البعيدة، فلاتكون العلة المتوسطة واسطة بين الفاعل ومنفعل ذلك الفاعل بل تكون واسطة بين فاعلها ومنفعلها كما صرح به أولا ، وحينئذ لا يحتاج في إخراجها عن تعريف الآلة إلى القيد الأخير، بل هي خارجة بقوله «ومنفعله» أي منفعل ذلك الفاعل. والجواب : أنا إذا فرضنا أن (أ) - مثلاً - أوجد (ب) و (ب) أوجد (ج)، فلاشك أن (أ) له مدخل في وجود (ج) وليس ذلك إلا لكونه فاعلاً له، إذ لا يمكن وجود (ج) إلا بأن يصير (أ) فاعلاً ل(ب) لكنه فاعل بعيد لم يصل أثره إلى (ج) فيكون (ج) أيضاً منفعلا له بعيداً، فيصدق على (ب) حينئذانه واسطة بين الفاعل ومنفعه في الجملة، فيحتاج إلى إخراجه بالقيد الأخير. وإلى ما ذكرناه مفصلاً أشار إجمالاً بقوله : «إذ علة علة الشيء علة له بالواسطة » - فتأمل (شريف).
The introduction. And they defined it as: a canonical tool whose observance protects the mind from error in thought. So the tool is the intermediary between the agent and its patient in the delivery of its effect to it, like the saw for the carpenter, for it is the intermediary between him and the wood in the delivery of his effect to it. So the last restriction is to exclude the intermediate cause, for it is an intermediary between its agent and its patient, since the cause of a thing's cause is a cause of that thing by mediation. For if (A) is a cause of (B), and (B) is a cause of (C), then (A) is a cause of (C) but mediated by (B). However, it is not an intermediary between them in the delivery of the effect of the remote cause to the effect, because the effect of the remote cause does not reach the effect, let alone that something else intervene in that. And by this art, both meanings of nutq are strengthened and manifested for the human soul called natiqa, so it is derived from nutq. (1) An objection was raised against it: On this account, the effect is not acted upon by the remote cause, so the intermediate cause is not an intermediary between the agent and its patient, but rather it is an intermediary between its own agent and its patient as stated initially. Then there is no need in excluding it from the definition of tool to the last restriction, rather it is excluded by His saying 'and its patient', i.e., the patient of that agent. The answer: If we suppose that (A), for example, brings (B) into existence and (B) brings (C) into existence, then there is no doubt that (A) has a role in the existence of (C), and that is only because it is its agent, since (C) cannot exist except by (A) being an agent of (B), but it is a remote agent whose effect does not reach (C), so (C) is also a remote patient of it. So (B) is then truly an intermediary between the agent and its patient in a general sense, so it needs to be excluded by the last restriction. And what we mentioned in detail was alluded to briefly by his saying 'the cause of a thing's cause is a cause of it by mediation.' - so reflect.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage provides the formal definition of logic as 'a canonical tool whose observance protects the mind from error in thought.' The commentary then explains the term 'tool' (ālah) by defining it as an intermediary between an agent and its patient through which the agent's effect reaches the patient, giving the example of a saw for a carpenter. The 'last restriction' (al-qayd al-ākhir) refers to the phrase 'in the delivery of its effect to it' to exclude intermediate causes that do not directly transmit the effect. The commentary clarifies that an intermediate cause, like (B) in the sequence A → B → C, is not an intermediary between A and C in the delivery of A's effect to C because A's effect does not reach C; rather, B mediates causation but not transmission. The objection argues that if the remote cause's effect does not reach the patient, then the intermediate cause should not be considered an intermediary between them, so the last restriction is unnecessary. The answer responds that the remote cause (A) does have a role in the existence of (C) as a remote agent, and therefore (B) still acts as an intermediary in a general sense, necessitating the last restriction. The passage ends by noting that the summary of this detailed explanation is alluded to in the statement 'the cause of a thing's cause is a cause of it by mediation.'

Key Terms
آلة
قانونية
علة متوسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the definition of logic as a tool (ālah) include the restriction 'in the delivery of its effect to it'?
A) To emphasize that logic is a law
B) To exclude intermediate causes that do not directly transmit the effect
C) To include all types of causes
D) To distinguish logic from other instruments

Key Takeaway
The definition of logic as a tool excludes intermediate causes because tools directly transmit the agent's effect, while intermediate causes mediate causation without transmitting the effect.

What Is Mantiq?

What is a Tool? The Definition of Mantiq and the Intermediate Cause Objection
Commentator defines mantiq as a tool, explains 'ala', and responds to an objection about intermediate causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the elements of the definition of 'āla' in the definition of mantiq and reconstruct the objection-response debate about intermediate causes.

Core Idea
A tool is an intermediary that transmits an agent's effect directly; the intermediate cause does not.

In this lesson, we delve into the first part of the definition of mantiq: 'a legal instrument whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking.' The commentator unpacks the term 'instrument' (āla) and clarifies its boundaries using a causal chain. Understanding this nuance is crucial for distinguishing genuine logical instruments from intermediate causes that share some features but lack direct transmission of effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the definition of mantiq
The definition is 'āla qānūniyya...' (a legal instrument...). Break it into parts: 'āla' (instrument), 'qānūniyya' (legal), and the description 'whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking.' focus on 'āla' as the key term.
The term 'legal' here refers to general rules (qawānīn) not fiqh.
2
Understand the definition of 'āla' with the carpenter example
The commentator defines 'āla' as an intermediary between agent and patient in transmission of effect. The saw is between carpenter (agent) and wood (patient). The effect (cutting) reaches wood through the saw.
3
Recognize the role of the last condition
The last condition 'in the transmission of its effect' (fī wuṣūl atharihi ilayhi) is needed to exclude intermediate causes. An intermediate cause like B between A and C is not a tool because A's effect does not directly reach C.
The commentator notes that the cause of a thing's cause is a cause for it indirectly, but not a tool.
4
Analyze the objector's argument and the response
The objector says: If B is not an intermediary between A and C, then the last condition is redundant. The responder shows that B is still an intermediary in some respect (since A has involvement in C's existence), so the condition is necessary.
This is a conditional argument: if not intermediary then redundant; but since it is intermediary in some respect, condition needed.
5
Apply the refined definition to a new example
Consider a chain: knowledge of the ruling (remote cause) leads to memorization (intermediate) which leads to practice (effect). Is memorization a tool or intermediate cause? Use the conditions from the passage to decide.
Islamic example: the role of hadith memorization in deriving rulings.

And they defined it as: a legal instrument whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking. The instrument is the intermediary between the agent and its patient in the transmission of its effect, like the saw for the carpenter, for it is an intermediary between him and the wood in the transmission of his effect. So the last condition is for excluding the intermediate cause, for it is an intermediary between its agent and its patient, since the cause of a thing's cause is a cause for that thing indirectly. For if (A) is a cause for (B) and (B) is a cause for (C), then (A) is a cause for (C) but through (B). However it is not an intermediary between them in the transmission of the remote cause's effect to the effect, because the effect of the remote cause does not reach the effect, let alone that something else mediates that. And through this art both meanings of speech become strong and manifest for the human soul called rational, so a name was derived for it from speech. (Sharif). Then there is no need in excluding it from the definition of instrument to the last condition; rather it is excluded by his saying 'and its patient', i.e., the patient of that agent. The answer: If we suppose that (A) for example brought about (B) and (B) brought about (C), then there is no doubt that (A) has an involvement in the existence of (C), and that is only because it is an agent for it, since the existence of (C) is only possible by (A) being an agent for (B), but it is a remote agent whose effect did not reach (C), so (C) is also a remote patient for it. Then (B) is truly an intermediary between the agent and its patient in some respect, so it needs to be excluded by the last condition. And to what we mentioned in detail, he alluded summarily in his saying: 'For the cause of a thing's cause is a cause for it indirectly' – so reflect (Sharif). Only that which reaches it is the effect of the intermediate cause because it emanates from it, and it (the intermediate) is from the remote.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of mantiq (logic). It begins by quoting the definition: 'a legal instrument whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking.' The commentator then unpacks the term 'instrument' (āla), explaining it as an intermediary between an agent and its patient in the transmission of the agent's effect. The example given is the saw as an intermediary between the carpenter and the wood. The 'last condition' in the definition refers to the phrase 'in the transmission of its effect' – this is needed to exclude intermediate causes. The commentator explains that an intermediate cause, like B between A and C, is not a direct intermediary for the remote cause's effect because the remote cause's effect does not reach the ultimate effect directly. However, an objection is raised: if we accept this, then the intermediate cause is not an intermediary between the remote agent and its patient, so it is already excluded by the phrase 'its patient' in the definition, making the last condition redundant. The response argues that the remote cause (A) does have an effect on the ultimate effect (C) indirectly, so the intermediate cause (B) is still an intermediary between A and C in some sense, hence the last condition is necessary to exclude it. The response is summarized by the statement 'the cause of a thing's cause is a cause for it indirectly.' This dialectical exchange guards against a misunderstanding of the definition of 'instrument' by clarifying that intermediate causes, though not direct tools, have a share in the causal chain and must be explicitly excluded.

The definition of 'āla explicitly excludes intermediate causes because they transmit only indirectly; the last condition is necessary to prevent confusion between a direct instrument and an indirect cause.

What is a Qanun? Universal Rules in Logic and Grammar
Commentator explains 'qanun' as a universal rule with a grammatical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a universal rule (qanun) and derive a particular ruling from it using categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A qanun is a universal proposition that contains its particulars in potentiality, actualized by syllogistic derivation.

In defining logic as an instrument, the commentator introduces 'qanun' as a universal rule that applies to all its particulars. Using the grammatical rule 'the subject is nominative' as an example, he demonstrates how a universal proposition serves as a major premise in a syllogism to yield specific rulings. This lesson breaks down the concept and its application in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qanun
Look at the Arabic: 'qanun' is described as 'amr kulli' (universal matter) that 'yanṭabiq ʿalā jamīʿ juzʾiyyātihi' (applies to all its particulars). Understand that it is a universal proposition, not a single term.
2
Analyze the grammatical example
The example: 'al-fāʿilu marfūʿ' (the subject is nominative). Notice that this is a universal proposition. Its subject 'al-fāʿilu' is a universal concept that applies to all subjects like Zayd, Amr, etc.
3
Understand potentiality (bi-l-quwwa)
The universal rule contains the rulings of its particulars in potentiality. The process of 'tafrīʿ' extracts these into actuality.
4
Construct the syllogism
The derivation uses a categorical syllogism: minor premise (e.g., 'Zayd is a subject'), major premise ('Every subject is nominative'), conclusion ('Zayd is nominative'). Identify the middle term and figure.
The middle term is 'subject', the figure is first (Barbara), and the mood is AAA, which is valid.
5
Apply the concept to other Islamic sciences
Realize that this structure applies to any universal principle, e.g., in usul al-fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' with minor 'Nabidh is intoxicant'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And 'the law' (al-qānūn) is a universal matter that applies to all its particulars to know their rulings from it: like the saying of grammarians: 'The subject is nominative (marfūʿ).' For it is a universal matter applying to all its particulars, by which the rulings of its particulars are known, such that from it it is known that 'Zayd' is nominative in our saying 'Zayd struck', for he is a subject. And logic is only called an 'instrument' because it is an intermediary between the rational faculty and the... (?) universal does not prevent... If you say for example: 'Every subject is nominative', then 'subject' is a universal matter – i.e., a concept whose very conception does not prevent participation in it – and it has many particulars upon which it is predicated as it is, and this proposition is also a universal matter, i.e., a universal proposition in which a ruling has been made on all the particulars of its subject. And it has branches that are the rulings occurring on the specifics of those particulars, like your saying: 'Zayd' in 'Zayd said' is nominative, and 'Amr' in 'Amr struck' is nominative – and so on. And these branches are subsumed under the universal proposition that includes them in potentiality close to actuality. And 'law', 'principle', 'rule', and 'norm' are names for this universal proposition in relation to those branches subsumed under it. And extracting them from it into actuality is called 'derivation' (tafrīʿ). That is by predicating its subject – i.e., 'subject' – on 'Zayd' for example, resulting in a proposition which is made the minor premise of the syllogism, and that universal proposition is the major premise, like this: 'Zayd is a subject' and 'Every subject is nominative' yields 'Zayd is nominative'. So by this operation this branch has been brought from potentiality to actuality. And measure to that others. So his saying 'a universal matter' means a universal proposition. And his saying 'applying' means potentially inclusive of its particulars: i.e., of all the rulings of the particulars of its subject. And his saying 'to know their rulings from it' means actually, in the manner we have explained (Sharif).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the term 'qānūn' (law/rule) from the definition of mantiq. The commentator defines 'qānūn' as a universal matter (amr kullī) that applies to all its particulars, enabling one to derive the rulings of those particulars. The example from Arabic grammar is the rule 'The subject (fāʿil) is nominative (marfūʿ).' This universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) subsumes all particular subjects like Zayd, Amr, etc. The commentator explains that this universal rule is potentially inclusive of its instances, and by predicating the subject term of the universal rule onto a particular (e.g., 'Zayd is a subject'), we form a syllogism: the universal rule serves as the major premise, the predication as the minor premise, and the conclusion is the particular ruling (e.g., 'Zayd is nominative'). This process is called tafrīʿ (derivation). The commentator also notes that terms like 'aṣl' (principle), 'qāʿida' (rule), and 'ḍābiṭ' (norm) are synonymous with 'qānūn' in this context. This explanation shows how a universal rule functions as a tool (āla) in logic, bridging the rational faculty and the object of inquiry. The example from nahw is appropriate as it is part of the Islamic sciences.

أمر كلّي
منطبق
تفريع

Checkpoint

A) By analogy with another subject.
B) By induction from multiple examples.
C) By categorical syllogism with the universal rule as major premise.
D) By immediate inference from the universal rule alone.

Key Takeaway
A qanun is a universal proposition that contains its particulars potentially; its actualization requires a categorical syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Logic as a Tool and a Law
Author defines logic and explains its nature as a tool and universal law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two aspects of logic's definition—tool and law—and explain how each functions in the acquisition of knowledge.

Core Idea
Logic is a tool between the mind and knowledge, and a universal law governing thought.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the classic definition of logic. He explains why it is called a 'tool' and a 'law', using concrete examples from grammar and logic conversion. Understanding this dual nature is essential for appreciating logic's role in Islamic sciences as both an instrument and a universal framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms used to define logic
The author uses 'tool' (آلة) and 'law' (قانون). Locate these terms in the Arabic and note how they are introduced.
The author calls logic both a tool and a law.
2
Understand 'tool' as intermediary
The author says logic is a tool between the rational faculty (القوة العاقلة) and the acquisition of unknown conclusions. Think of it as a means to move from known premises to new knowledge.
3
Understand 'law' as universal principle
The author illustrates with a grammatical rule: 'the subject is marfu'. This universal applies to every particular subject. Similarly, logic's rules are universal laws applying to all reasoning across sciences.
The example shows how a universal rule yields specific rulings.
4
Recognize the condition of protection
The author emphasizes that it is the observance (مراعاتها) of logic, not logic itself, that protects the mind from error. Why is this distinction important?
This clarifies that a logician can still err if they neglect the tool.
5
Connect to Islamic sciences
This definition shows logic's utility in disciplines like kalam and usul al-fiqh, where universal principles guide specific rulings.
For example, the universal 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is a law applying to all intoxicants.

Only the effect of the intermediate cause reaches it because it originates from it and is from the distant cause. And the 'law' is a universal matter that applies to all its particulars so that their rulings may be known from it: like the statement of the grammarians: 'the subject is in the nominative case' — for it is a universal matter applying to all its particulars, and one knows the rulings of its particulars from it, so that from it one knows that 'Zayd' is in the nominative in our saying 'Zayd struck' because he is the subject. And logic was only [called] a 'tool' because it is an intermediary between the rational faculty and the acquired objects of inquiry in acquisition. And it was only [called] a 'law' because its issues are universal laws applying to all their particulars, just as if we know that the necessary negative converts to a perpetually negative, we know from it that our statement 'Nothing of man is a stone necessarily' converts to our statement 'Nothing of stone is a man perpetually'. And he only said 'the observance of which protects the mind' because logic itself does not protect the mind from error; otherwise no logician would ever commit error; but it is not so, for perhaps he errs due to neglecting the tool. This is the meaning of the definition. As for its qualifications, 'tool' serves as the genus and 'lawlike' as the differentia, excluding particular tools of the craftsmen, and his statement 'the observance of which protects the mind from error in thought' excludes the lawlike sciences the observance of which does not protect the mind from error in thought — rather in statement — like the Arabic sciences. And this definition was only a rasm because its being a tool is an accident among its accidents, for the essential of a thing is only what belongs to it in itself, and the tool-ness of logic is not for it in itself, but in relation to other sciences of wisdom, and because it is a definition by the end, since the end (1) It was objected to him: the rational faculty is receptive of acquired objects — not active for them. And it was answered: if judgment is an act then there is no problem in assents, and if it is a perception, then its being a tool is either based on what is apparent and readily understood by beginners that the rational faculty is active for its perceptions — as he mentioned — or based on its being a tool between the rational faculty and the informations whose arrangement is for acquiring unknowns, for the effect occurring in them by the faculty's arranging them correctly is only through this art (Sharif).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of logic (mantiq). It begins by explaining the term 'law' (qanun) as a universal rule applicable to all particulars, using the grammatical rule 'the subject is in the nominative' as an example. From this universal, one can deduce the specific ruling for a particular subject like Zayd. The author then clarifies why logic is called a 'tool' (ala): it mediates between the rational faculty and the acquisition of unknown conclusions. It is also called a 'law' because its principles are universal, e.g., the conversion of necessary negative propositions. The text emphasizes that it is the observance (mura'ah) of logic that protects the mind, not logic itself, because logicians can still err by neglecting the tool. The commentator then analyzes the definition's qualifications (ihtirazat): 'tool' serves as genus, 'lawlike' (qanuniyyah) as differentia, excluding specific tools of craftsmen, and 'observance protects the mind from error in thought' excludes other lawlike sciences like Arabic grammar, which protect only from error in statement (maqal) not thought. Finally, the author explains why this definition is a rasm (descriptive definition) rather than a hadd (essential definition): being a tool is an accidental attribute relative to other sciences, and it defines by the goal (ghayah). An objection and answer follow regarding the rational faculty being receptive rather than active, with the response that either judgment is an act (making the faculty active) or it is perception, in which case the tool status is either apparent to beginners or based on the tool's role in arranging information correctly.

Key Terms
آلة
قانون
قوة عاقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is logic called a 'law' (qanun)?
A) Because it is derived from divine legislation.
B) Because it consists of universal rules that apply to all their particulars.
C) Because it is required for all Muslims.
D) Because it is taught in law schools.

Key Takeaway
Logic is defined as a tool (for acquiring knowledge) and a law (universal principle), and it is the observance of logic that protects the mind from error.

Why the Definition of Logic is a Descriptive Definition (Rasm)
Author defines logic and explains its nature as a tool and universal law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of logic is a rasm (descriptive definition) rather than a hadd (essential definition), using the concepts of accident and goal.

Core Idea
The definition of logic is descriptive because being a tool is accidental and the definition uses the goal.

The commentator delves into the structure of the definition itself. He explains that 'tool' serves as a genus and 'lawlike' as a differentia, but this is not an essential definition. Instead, it is a rasm because the attribute of being a tool is accidental to logic and the definition is by the end (ghayah). This distinction is crucial for understanding the precision of definitions in logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the definition's components
The commentator identifies 'tool' as genus (جنس) and 'lawlike' as differentia (فصل). Locate this in the Arabic: 'فالآلة بمنزلة الجنس و القانونية بمنزلة الفصل'.
This is a grammatical analysis of the definition.
2
Understand the concept of accident (عرض)
The author says being a tool is an accident (عارض) of logic. An accident is a property that belongs to a thing not in itself but in relation to something else.
Essential (ذاتي) belongs to the thing in itself, while accidental is external.
3
Distinguish essential vs. accidental
The commentator contrasts: 'the essential of a thing is only what belongs to it in itself, and tool-ness of logic is not for it in itself, but in relation to other sciences.' Why is tool-ness relational?
Logic is a tool for other sciences, not for itself.
4
Identify definition by goal (غاية)
The author says the definition is by the end (ghayah). The goal of logic is to protect the mind from error. This makes it a definition by final cause, not by essence.
5
Evaluate the definition's completeness
The definition excludes other tools (by lawlikeness) and other lawlike sciences (by protection of thought). Check if it successfully distinguishes logic from everything else.
As a rasm, it is complete if it uniquely identifies logic.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما الواصل إليه أثر العلة المتوسطة لأنه الصادر منها وهي من البعيدة. و «القانون» أمر كلّي ينطبق على جميع جزئياته ليتعرف أحكامها منه : كقول النحاة : الفاعل مرفوع» فإنّه أمر كلّي منطبق على جميع جزئياته، يتعرف أحكام جزئياته منه، حتى يتعرف منه أن «زيداً» مرفوع في قولنا : «ضرب زيد» فإنّه فاعل. و إنما كان المنطق «آلة» لأنه واسطة بين القوّة العاقلة، وبين المطالب الكسبية في الاكتساب ، وإنما كان «قانوناً» لأن مسائله قوانين كلية منطبقة على سائر جزئياتها، كما إذا عرفنا أن السالبة الضرورية تنعكس إلى سالبة دائمة، عرفنا منه أن قولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة» ينعكس إلى قولنا : «لاشيء من الحجر بإنسان دائماً». و إنما قال : تعصم مراعاتها الذهن لأن المنطق ليس هو نفسه يعصم الذهن عن الخطأ، وإلا لم يعرض للمنطقي خطأ أصلاً؛ وليس كذلك، فإنه ربما يخطيء لإهمال الآلة. هذا مفهوم التعريف ؛ و أما احترازاته فـ«الآلة» بمنزلة الجنس و «القانونية» بمنزلة الفصل يخرج الآلات الجزئية لأرباب الصنائع، وقوله : تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطا في الفكر» يخرج العلوم القانونية التي لا تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الضلال في الفكر - بل في المقال – كالعلوم العربية. وإنما كان هذا التعريف رسماً لأن كونه آلة عارض من عوارضه، فإنّ الذاتي للشيء إنما يكون له في نفسه، و الآلية للمنطق ليست له في نفسه، بل بالقياس إلى غيره من العلوم الحكميّة، ولأنه تعريف بالغاية، إذ غاية (1) قيل عليه : إن القوة العاقلة قابلة للمطالب الكسبية - لا فاعلة لها. واجيب : بأن الحكم إن كان فعلاً فلا إشكال في التصديقات، وإن كان إدراكاً فكونه آلة إما بناء على الظاهر المتبادر إلى أفهام المبتدئين من كون العاقلة فاعلة لإدراكاتها - كما ذكره - وإما بناء على أنه آلة بين القوة العاقلة وبين المعلومات التي ترتيبها لاكتساب المجهولات، فإن الأثر الحاصل فيها بترتيب العاقلة إياها على وجه الصواب إنما هو بواسطة هذا الفن (شريف).
Only the effect of the intermediate cause reaches it because it originates from it and is from the distant cause. And the 'law' is a universal matter that applies to all its particulars so that their rulings may be known from it: like the statement of the grammarians: 'the subject is in the nominative case' — for it is a universal matter applying to all its particulars, and one knows the rulings of its particulars from it, so that from it one knows that 'Zayd' is in the nominative in our saying 'Zayd struck' because he is the subject. And logic was only [called] a 'tool' because it is an intermediary between the rational faculty and the acquired objects of inquiry in acquisition. And it was only [called] a 'law' because its issues are universal laws applying to all their particulars, just as if we know that the necessary negative converts to a perpetually negative, we know from it that our statement 'Nothing of man is a stone necessarily' converts to our statement 'Nothing of stone is a man perpetually'. And he only said 'the observance of which protects the mind' because logic itself does not protect the mind from error; otherwise no logician would ever commit error; but it is not so, for perhaps he errs due to neglecting the tool. This is the meaning of the definition. As for its qualifications, 'tool' serves as the genus and 'lawlike' as the differentia, excluding particular tools of the craftsmen, and his statement 'the observance of which protects the mind from error in thought' excludes the lawlike sciences the observance of which does not protect the mind from error in thought — rather in statement — like the Arabic sciences. And this definition was only a rasm because its being a tool is an accident among its accidents, for the essential of a thing is only what belongs to it in itself, and the tool-ness of logic is not for it in itself, but in relation to other sciences of wisdom, and because it is a definition by the end, since the end (1) It was objected to him: the rational faculty is receptive of acquired objects — not active for them. And it was answered: if judgment is an act then there is no problem in assents, and if it is a perception, then its being a tool is either based on what is apparent and readily understood by beginners that the rational faculty is active for its perceptions — as he mentioned — or based on its being a tool between the rational faculty and the informations whose arrangement is for acquiring unknowns, for the effect occurring in them by the faculty's arranging them correctly is only through this art (Sharif).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of logic (mantiq). It begins by explaining the term 'law' (qanun) as a universal rule applicable to all particulars, using the grammatical rule 'the subject is in the nominative' as an example. From this universal, one can deduce the specific ruling for a particular subject like Zayd. The author then clarifies why logic is called a 'tool' (ala): it mediates between the rational faculty and the acquisition of unknown conclusions. It is also called a 'law' because its principles are universal, e.g., the conversion of necessary negative propositions. The text emphasizes that it is the observance (mura'ah) of logic that protects the mind, not logic itself, because logicians can still err by neglecting the tool. The commentator then analyzes the definition's qualifications (ihtirazat): 'tool' serves as genus, 'lawlike' (qanuniyyah) as differentia, excluding specific tools of craftsmen, and 'observance protects the mind from error in thought' excludes other lawlike sciences like Arabic grammar, which protect only from error in statement (maqal) not thought. Finally, the author explains why this definition is a rasm (descriptive definition) rather than a hadd (essential definition): being a tool is an accidental attribute relative to other sciences, and it defines by the goal (ghayah). An objection and answer follow regarding the rational faculty being receptive rather than active, with the response that either judgment is an act (making the faculty active) or it is perception, in which case the tool status is either apparent to beginners or based on the tool's role in arranging information correctly.

The Objection: Is the Rational Faculty Active or Receptive?
Author defines logic and explains its nature as a tool and universal law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the objection regarding the rational faculty's role and evaluate the commentator's response.

Core Idea
The rational faculty is both receptive and active; logic serves as tool for correct arrangement of perceptions.

In the final part of the passage, the commentators address an objection: if the rational faculty is merely receptive, how can logic be a tool for acquisition? The response clarifies that depending on the nature of judgments (acts or perceptions), the tool role holds. This subtle dialectic deepens our understanding of logic's function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (الاعتراض)
The objector says the rational faculty is receptive (قابلة) of acquired objects, not active (فاعلة). Locate the phrase: 'قيل عليه: إن القوة العاقلة قابلة للمطالب الكسبية - لا فاعلة لها.' This challenges the notion of logic as a tool for acquisition.
If the faculty only receives, how can it actively acquire?
2
Find the two-part answer
The responder gives two scenarios: if judgment is an act (فعل), then it is active in assents (تصديقات). If judgment is perception (إدراك), then tool-ness is based on common understanding or the faculty's arranging role.
The answer splits the issue based on the nature of judgment.
3
Analyze the distinction between act and perception
Consider: In assents (e.g., affirming a proposition), the faculty seems active. In concepts (tasawwur), it arranges perceptions. How does this distinction help?
4
Assess the strength of the answer
Does the response fully address the objection? It offers two defenses: either the faculty is active in assents, or else in arrangement it is active. So the tool role remains valid.
The answer covers both possibilities.
5
Connect to the definition of logic
If the answer is accepted, the definition of logic as a tool stands. This shows that logicians anticipated and resolved philosophical challenges to their discipline.

Why Logic is an Instrumental Law and its Rasm Definition
Author defines logic and analyzes its definition as a rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus, differentia, and rasm nature of the classical definition of logic, and distinguish between essential and descriptive definitions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Logic's definition is a descriptive rasm, not an essential hadd, because it uses accidental and external features.

In this passage, the author explains why logic is called a law, why it safeguards the mind only when used, and why its definition is a rasm (description) rather than a hadd (true definition). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh, where the difference between essential and accidental properties can determine the soundness of an argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition components
Locate the three parts of the definition: instrument (āla), law (qānūn), and safeguarding (ʻiṣma). Note how each part functions in the definition.
The definition is: 'Logic is an instrumental law whose observation safeguards the mind from error in thought.'
2
Understand why it's called a law
The author says logic is a law because its rules are universal, applying to all particulars. Study the example given: the conversion of a necessary negative to a perpetual negative.
Example: 'Nothing of man is a stone necessarily' converts to 'Nothing of stone is a man perpetually.'
3
Analyze the exclusion clauses
The term 'instrument' acts as a genus, excluding particular tools of crafts. The phrase 'safeguards the mind from error in thought' excludes sciences like grammar that safeguard speech.
This shows the definition is precise but uses accidental features.
4
Determine why it is a rasm
The author gives two reasons: instrumentality is accidental (not essential to logic), and the definition uses the goal (safeguarding) which is external. Both make it a rasm.
A hadd would capture the essence; a rasm uses attributes or goals.
