⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما كان «قانوناً» لأن مسائله قوانين كلية منطبقة على سائر جزئياتها، كما إذا عرفنا أن السالبة الضرورية تنعكس إلى سالبة دائمة، عرفنا منه أن قولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة» ينعكس إلى قولنا : «لاشيء من الحجر بإنسان دائماً». و إنما قال : تعصم مراعاتها الذهن لأن المنطق ليس هو نفسه يعصم الذهن عن الخطأ، وإلا لم يعرض للمنطقي خطأ أصلاً؛ وليس كذلك، فإنه ربما يخطيء لإهمال الآلة. هذا مفهوم التعريف ؛ و أما احترازاته فـ«الآلة» بمنزلة الجنس و «القانونية» بمنزلة الفصل يخرج الآلات الجزئية لأرباب الصنائع، وقوله : تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطا في الفكر» يخرج العلوم القانونية التي لا تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الضلال في الفكر - بل في المقال – كالعلوم العربية. وإنما كان هذا التعريف رسماً لأن كونه آلة عارض من عوارضه، فإنّ الذاتي للشيء إنما يكون له في نفسه، و الآلية للمنطق ليست له في نفسه، بل بالقياس إلى غيره من العلوم الحكميّة، ولأنه تعريف بالغاية، إذ غاية المنطق العصمة عن الخطإ في الفكر، وغاية الشيء تكون خارجة عنه، والتعريف بالخارج رسم. 1) قيل عليه : إن القوة العاقلة قابلة للمطالب الكسبية - لا فاعلة لها. واجيب : بأن الحكم إن كان فعلاً فلا إشكال في التصديقات، وإن كان إدراكاً فكونه آلة إما بناء على الظاهر المتبادر إلى أفهام المبتدئين من كون العاقلة فاعلة لإدراكاتها - كما ذكره - وإما بناء على أنه آلة بين القوة العاقلة وبين المعلومات التي ترتيبها لاكتساب المجهولات، فإن الأثر الحاصل فيها بترتيب العاقلة إياها على وجه الصواب إنما هو بواسطة هذا الفن (شريف).
And it is called 'law' because its issues are universal laws applicable to all their particulars, as when we know that the necessary negative converts to a perpetual negative, we know from it that our statement 'Nothing of man is a stone necessarily' converts to our statement 'Nothing of stone is a man perpetually'. And he said: 'observing it safeguards the mind' because logic itself does not safeguard the mind from error, otherwise no logician would ever err; and that is not the case, for he may err due to neglecting the instrument. This is the meaning of the definition. As for its exclusions: 'instrument' is like the genus and 'legal' is like the differentia, excluding the particular instruments of practitioners of crafts; and his statement 'observing it safeguards the mind from error in thought' excludes the legal sciences that do not safeguard the mind from error in thought - rather in discourse - like the Arabic sciences. And this definition was a rasm because being an instrument is an accident among its accidents; for the essential of a thing is only for it in itself, and instrumentality for logic is not for it in itself, but by relation to other philosophical sciences; and because it is a definition by the goal, for the goal of logic is safeguarding from error in thought, and the goal of a thing is external to it, and definition by the external is a rasm. 1) It was objected to him: The rational faculty is receptive to acquired demands - not an agent of them. And answered: If judgment is an act, then there is no problem in affirmations; and if it is perception, then its being an instrument is either based on the apparent meaning that occurs to beginners that the rational faculty is an agent of its perceptions - as he mentioned - or based on its being an instrument between the rational faculty and the information whose arrangement is for acquiring unknowns, for the effect obtained in it by arrangement of the rational faculty for them in a correct manner is only by means of this art (Sharif).
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of logic. It begins by explaining why logic is called a law: because its principles are universal and apply to all particulars. The author gives an example from the conversion of a necessary negative proposition. Then he clarifies the phrase 'observing it safeguards the mind' — logic itself is not infallible; it only prevents error when properly used, but negligence can lead to error. Next, he analyzes the components of the definition: 'instrument' is the genus, and 'legal' is the differentia, which excludes particular tools of crafts. The phrase 'safeguards the mind from error in thought' excludes sciences like grammar that safeguard only speech. Finally, he explains why this definition is a rasm (description), not a hadd (true definition): being an instrument is accidental to logic, since its essence is not inherently instrumental but relative to other sciences; also, it defines by the goal (safeguarding from error), which is external. The passage includes an objection: the rational faculty is receptive, not an agent. The answer distinguishes: if judgment is an act, there is no issue; if perception, logic serves as an instrument between the rational faculty and information, based on common understanding or its role in arranging perceptions correctly.

آلة
قانون
رسم
عصمة

Key Takeaway
The definition of logic as an instrumental law safeguarding the mind is a rasm because it uses accidental properties and an external goal, not the essence of logic.

Definition by External Goal: Rasm vs Hadd
Author defines logic as immunity from error and notes that definition by external goal is a rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a hadd (essential definition) from a rasm (descriptive definition) and identify which type is used for the definition of logic in this passage.

Core Idea
A definition by external goal is a rasm, not a hadd.

In this opening of the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, the author defines logic as 'immunity from error in thought' and immediately classifies this definition as a rasm. Why does he do that? Understanding the difference between a complete essential definition (hadd) and a descriptive definition (rasm) is crucial for evaluating definitions across the Islamic sciences. This lesson will help you parse that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-mantiq' (logic). Locate it in the passage.
2
Locate the definition given
The author states: 'Logic is immunity from error in thought'. This is the definition (rasm).
3
Determine if it is a hadd or rasm
The author says: 'the goal of a thing is external to it, and definition by what is external is a rasm'. The definition uses the goal (avoiding error), which is external, so it is a rasm.
4
Check what a hadd would look like
A hadd of logic would give its genus (e.g., 'a science' or 'a tool') and differentia (e.g., 'that safeguards the mind from error'). The author does not provide that here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح الرسالة الشمسية المنطق العصمة عن الخطإ في الفكر، وغاية الشيء تكون خارجة عنه، والتعريف بالخارج رسم.
Commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya: Logic is immunity from error in thought, and the goal of a thing is external to it, and definition by what is external is a description (rasm).
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage opens the commentary by defining logic. The author states that logic is 'immunity from error in thought' (al-‘ismah ‘an al-khata’ fi al-fikr). He then notes that the goal (ghayah) of a thing is external to it, and defining something by its external goal makes the definition a rasm (description) rather than a hadd (essential definition). This distinction is foundational: a hadd captures the essence, while a rasm captures accidental properties or external ends. Here, because logic's purpose is to avoid error, the definition is given as a rasm. In the Islamic sciences, the hadd of a discipline is its formal object and subject matter, while the rasm often points to its utility.

العصمة
رسم

Key Takeaway
A definition that captures an external goal is a rasm, not a hadd; always check whether the definiens refers to the essence or to an external property.

Logic's Twofold Nature: A Priori and Acquired
Argument that logic is partly a priori and partly acquired to avoid regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that logic cannot be entirely acquired and explain why some logical principles must be self-evident.

Core Idea
Logic needs a self-evident foundation to avoid an infinite regress of justifications.

Some claim logic is unnecessary because it is self-evident. Others say it is entirely learned. Both extremes face problems. This passage navigates between them, showing that logic is partly a priori (badihi) and partly acquired (nazari). The key move is a reductio argument: if all logic were acquired, you would need a prior logic to acquire it, leading to circularity or infinite regress. Therefore, some logical principles must be a priori.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection is that logic is entirely a priori, so there is no need to learn it. This would make the study of logic pointless. Find this in the commentary where it says 'Logic is a priori, so there is no need to learn it.'
2
Understand the reductio against 'wholly acquired'
The passage argues that if logic were entirely acquired (nazari), then acquiring it would require another logical method, which would itself require another, leading to either circularity (using logic to learn logic) or infinite regress (an endless chain of prior logics). Both are impossible.
This is a standard argument against foundationalism without self-evident starting points.
3
Extract the conclusion
Since neither extreme works, logic must be a mixture: some parts are self-evident (badihi) and other parts are derived from them (nazari). This makes learning logic both possible and necessary.
The badihi parts serve as the foundation that stops the regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وليس كله بديهياً - وإلا لاستغني عن تعلمه – ولا نظرياً، وإلا لدار أو تسلسل؛ بل بعضه بديهي وبعضه نظري مستفاد منه. القول : هذا إشارة إلى جواب معارضة تورد هاهنا، وتوجيهها أن يقال : «المنطق بديهي فلا حاجة إلى تعلمه». بيان الأول أنه لو لم يكن المنطق بديهياً لكان كسبياً، فاحتيج في تحصيله إلى قانون آخر، وذلك القانون أيضاً يحتاج إلى قانون آخر، فإما أن يدور الاكتساب أو يتسلسل، وهما محالان.
He said: And it is not entirely a priori – otherwise one would be independent of learning it – nor entirely acquired, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would occur; rather, some of it is a priori and some acquired from it. The statement: This is an indication of the answer to an objection that is raised here, and its direction is that it is said: 'Logic is a priori, so there is no need to learn it.' The explanation of the first is that if logic were not a priori, it would be acquired, so it would need another law to acquire it, and that law would also need another law, so either the acquisition would be circular or regress infinitely, both of which are impossible.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defends the necessity of learning logic. The matn states that logic is neither wholly a priori (badihi) nor wholly acquired (nazari), but a mixture. The commentator explains that this answers an objection that logic is a priori and thus needs no learning. The proof: if logic were entirely acquired, it would require a prior logical method to acquire it, leading to circularity or infinite regress. Hence, some parts must be a priori. This is a standard argument against the claim that logic is unnecessary because it is self-evident.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
Logic must contain self-evident principles to avoid infinite regress; therefore it is partly a priori and partly acquired.

The Two Stations: Need for Logic Itself vs. Need to Learn It
Author distinguishes two stations and answers initial objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the need for logic itself and the need to learn it, and explain how this distinction defuses objections about logic being self-evident.

Core Idea
The proof establishes need for logic itself, not necessarily need to learn it.

The commentator begins by separating two distinct questions: do we need logic as a tool for reasoning, and do we need to study it formally? This distinction is critical because an opponent might argue that if logic's principles are self-evident, there is no need to compile or learn them. The author shows that even if logic is self-evident, the need for its use remains, and compilation serves important purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Stations
Locate in the Arabic where the author says 'هاهنا مقامين'. State what each station is: the need for logic itself (al-ihtiyaj ila nafs al-mantiq) and the need to learn it (al-ihtiyaj ila ta'allumihi).
The proof only addresses the first station.
2
Analyse the Scope of the Proof
The author says the proof (dalil) only establishes the need for logic itself, not the need to learn it. Why? Because even if logic is self-evident, it is still needed for acquiring theoretical sciences.
This is the key defensive move.
3
Examine the First Objection
The opponent asks: if logic topics are self-evident (badihi), why compile them in books? The author gives two benefits: removing hiddenness (izalat al-khafa') and enabling the acquisition of other acquired topics.
Compilation is not for teaching what is already known, but for clarifying and extending knowledge.
4
Examine the Second Objection
The opponent then says: deriving acquired parts from self-evident parts requires reflection, and that reflection needs another rule (law), leading to circularity. The author replies that the reflection itself is self-evident, so no separate law is needed.
This stops the regress without needing another level of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هاهنا مقامين: الأول الاحتياج إلى نفس المنطق، والثاني الاحتياج إلى تعلمه؛ والدليل إنما ينتهض على ثبوت الاحتياج إليه، لا إلى تعلمه. والمعارضة المذكورة وإن فرضنا إتمامها لا تدل إلا على الاستغناء عن تعلم المنطق، وهو لا يناقض الاحتياج إليه، فلا يبعد أن لا يحتاج إلى تعلم المنطق لكونه ضرورياً بجميع أجزائه أو لكونه معلوماً بشيء آخر، وتكون الحاجة ماسة إليه نفسه في تحصيل العلوم النظرية؛ فالمذكور في معرض المعارضة لا يصلح للمعارضة، لأنها المقابلة.
فإن قلت: إذا كانت هذه المباحث بديهية فلا حاجة إلى تدوينها في الكتب. قلت: في تدوينها في الكتب فائدتان: إحداهما إزالة ما عسى أن يكون في بعضها من خفاء محوج إلى التنبيه، وثانيتهما أن يتوصل بها إلى المباحث الأخرى الكسبية.
فإن قيل: استفادة البعض الكسبي من البعض البديهي إنما تكون بطريق النظر، فيحتاج في معرفة ذلك النظر إلى قانون آخر فيعود المحذور. قلنا: ذلك النظر أيضاً بديهي، فالكسبي من المنطق مستفاد من البديهي منه بطريق بديهي، فلا حاجة إلى قانون آخر أصلاً.
قيل عليه: إنما يلزم ذلك إذا قرر كلام المعارض على ما وجهه به، ولنا أن نقرره هكذا: لو كان المنطق محتاجاً إليه، لكان إما بديهياً أو كسبياً، وكلاهما باطل: أما الأول فلأنه يلزم الاستغناء عن تعلمه - وليس كذلك - وأما الثاني فللزوم الدور أو التسلسل في تحصيله. وعلى هذا فقد دلت المعارضة على نفي الاحتياج إلى المنطق نفسه، وحينئذ يجاب بذلك الجواب.
ورد: بأن إبطال كونه بديهياً أو كسبياً يدل على انتفائه في نفسه، ولا تعلق له بكونه محتاجاً إليه أو غير محتاج إليه، إذ يصح أن يقال: ليس المنطق شيئاً لا يحتاج إليه - وإلا لكان إما بديهياً أو كسبياً - وكلاهما باطل - فوجب أن يكون محتاجاً إليه. فظهر أن هذه شبهة يتمسك بها في نفي هذا العلم سواء احتيج إليه أو لم يحتج.
ولنا أيضا أن نقول في تقرير المعارضة: المنطق كسبي، فلا يحتاج إليه في اكتساب النظريات المحتاجة إلى المنطق: أما الأول فلأنه لو لم يكن كسبياً لكان بديهياً - وهو باطل، وإلا لاستغني عن تعلمه - وأما الثاني فلأنه لو احتيج إليه مع كونه كسبياً لزم الدور أو التسلسل. ولم يلتفت الشارح إلى هذا التقرير إذ كان المناسب حينئذ أن يقدم المصنف ذكر النظري وأن يشير إلى لزوم الدور أو التسلسل في اكتساب النظريات المحتاجة إلى المنطق، لا أن يقتصر على لزومهما في تحصيله في نفسه.
ويمكن أن يقال: لما بين المصنف الاحتياج إلى المنطق نفسه أراد أن يبين أن حاله ماذا: هل هو بديهي بجميع أجزائه حتى يستغنى عن تدوينه في الكتب، أو هو كسبي بجميع أجزائه حتى يمتنع تحصيله - فضلاً عن تدوينه – وبين فساد القسمين، فظهر أن المنطق ليس مما يستغنى عن تدوينه ولا مما يمتنع تحصيله وتدوينه مع كونه محتاجاً إليه، فوجب أن يدون في الكتب. ولم يلتفت الشارح أيضا إلى هذا التوجيه لأن المشهور في كتب الفن إيراد المعارضة في هذا الموضع لنفي الاحتياج إليه.
يعني أن المعارضة مقابلة الدليل بدليل آخر ممانع للأول في ثبوت مقتضاه، وما ذكرتم ليس كذلك.
Know that there are two stations: first, the need for logic itself; second, the need to learn it. The proof only establishes the need for it, not the need to learn it. The mentioned objection, even if we assume it is complete, only indicates independence from learning logic, and this does not contradict the need for it. So it is not unlikely that one does not need to learn logic because it is necessary in all its parts or because it is known through something else, while the need for it itself is urgent in acquiring theoretical sciences. Therefore what is mentioned in the context of objection is not suitable as an objection, because objection is a counter-argument.
If you say: If these topics are self-evident, there is no need to compile them in books. I say: Compiling them in books has two benefits: one is to remove any hiddenness that may require attention, and the second is that through them one may reach other acquired topics.
If it is said: Deriving some acquired parts from some self-evident parts is only by way of reflection, so knowing that reflection requires another law, returning the problem. We say: That reflection is also self-evident, so the acquired part of logic is derived from its self-evident part by a self-evident way, so there is no need for another law at all.
It was objected to him: That follows only if the opponent's speech is formulated as he formulated it. We can formulate it thus: If logic were needed, it would be either self-evident or acquired, and both are false. As for the first, because it would imply independence from learning it - and that is not so. As for the second, because of the occurrence of circularity or infinite regress in acquiring it. And on this basis, the objection indicates the negation of the need for logic itself, and then it is answered with that answer.
And it was replied: That invalidating its being self-evident or acquired indicates its negation in itself, and it has no connection to its being needed or not needed, because it is valid to say: 'Logic is not something that is not needed - otherwise it would be either self-evident or acquired - and both are false - so it must be needed.' Thus it becomes clear that this is a sophism used to negate this science whether it is needed or not.
We can also say in formulating the objection: Logic is acquired, so it is not needed in acquiring the theoretical matters that need logic. As for the first, because if it were not acquired it would be self-evident - which is false, otherwise one would be independent of learning it. As for the second, because if it were needed while being acquired, circularity or infinite regress would occur. And the commentator did not pay attention to this formulation, for the appropriate thing then would be for the author to mention the theoretical and to indicate the occurrence of circularity or infinite regress in acquiring the theoretical matters that need logic, not to restrict to their occurrence in acquiring it itself.
It can be said: When the author established the need for logic itself, he wanted to explain what its state is: Is it self-evident in all its parts so that one can do without compiling it in books, or is it acquired in all its parts so that acquiring it is impossible - let alone compiling it - and he showed the falsity of both parts, so it became clear that logic is not something that can be dispensed with by compiling it nor impossible to acquire and compile while being needed, so it must be compiled in books. And the commentator also did not pay attention to this orientation, because the common practice in the books of the art is to present the objection in this place to negate the need for it.
It means that the objection is a counter-argument with another argument that prevents the first in establishing its consequence, and what you mentioned is not like that.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This is a dialectical passage defending the need for logic against a series of objections. The author (the commentator on Qutbi) begins by distinguishing two stations: the need for logic itself and the need to learn it. The proof (dalil) only establishes the need for logic itself, not the need to learn it. An objection that logic is self-evident and thus does not need to be learned is then addressed. Even if true, this only shows independence from learning, not that logic itself is unnecessary. The author then answers an objection: if the topics of logic are self-evident, why compile them? He gives two benefits: removing hiddenness and enabling acquisition of other acquired topics. A further objection is raised: deriving acquired parts from self-evident parts requires reflection, which would need another law, leading to circularity. The author responds that reflection itself is self-evident, so no separate law is needed. The opponent then reformulates the objection as a dilemma: if logic is needed, it must be either self-evident or acquired; both are false; therefore logic is not needed. The author refutes this by showing that the dilemma actually proves the need for logic, because if logic were neither self-evident nor acquired, it would be something not needed, but that is absurd. Another formulation is proposed: logic is acquired, so it cannot be needed for acquiring theoretical matters (otherwise circularity). The commentator notes that the author (the original matn author) may have had a different intention: to show that logic is neither entirely self-evident (so it needs compilation) nor entirely acquired (so it is impossible), but rather it is needed and can be compiled. The passage ends by clarifying that a valid objection (mu'arada) must counter the proof's conclusion directly, not merely address peripheral issues. Throughout, the key distinction is between need for logic itself and need to learn it, and between self-evident and acquired knowledge.

Key Terms
مَنطِق
بَدِيهِيّ
كَسْبِيّ
مُعَارَضَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the proof (dalil) establish?
A) The need to learn logic
B) The need for logic itself
C) Both the need for logic itself and the need to learn it
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The proof for logic's necessity only directly supports the need for logic as a reasoning tool, not the need for formal instruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting the Dilemma: Logic as Self-Evident or Acquired
Author refutes the dilemma and discusses the role of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the opponent's dilemma (self-evident vs. acquired) and refute it by showing that it actually proves the need for logic.

Core Idea
The opponent's dilemma backfires: if logic were neither self-evident nor acquired, it must be needed.

The opponent sharpens the objection into a crisp dilemma: if logic is needed, it must be either self-evident (badihi) or acquired (kasbi). Both are supposedly false, so logic cannot be needed. The commentator shows this argument can be inverted to prove the opposite. Moreover, he clarifies the author's deeper intention: to demonstrate that logic is partly self-evident and partly acquired, making it both needed and compilable.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Opponent's Dilemma
Find in the Arabic where the opponent says: if logic is needed, it is either badihi or kasbi. Then both are false, so logic is not needed. Write the conditional form.
This is a classic destructive dilemma.
2
Identify the Refutation
The author argues that the opponent's premises actually lead to the conclusion that logic is needed. How? If logic were not needed, it would be either badihi or kasbi, but both are false, so it must be needed. This is a logical inversion.
The opponent's own argument is turned against them.
3
Understand the Commentator's Alternative Formulation
The commentator offers another formulation: logic is acquired, so it cannot be needed for acquiring theoretical matters (circularity). But he notes the author's intention may have been different: to show that logic is neither entirely badihi nor entirely kasbi, hence it can be compiled.
This clarifies the author's purpose in presenting the dilemma.
4
Assess the Overall Dialectic
The passage ends by defining mu'arada (objection) as a counter-argument that directly opposes the proof's conclusion. The opponent's initial objection does not meet this criterion because it only challenges peripheral issues.
This emphasizes the importance of precise dialectical engagement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هاهنا مقامين: الأول الاحتياج إلى نفس المنطق، والثاني الاحتياج إلى تعلمه؛ والدليل إنما ينتهض على ثبوت الاحتياج إليه، لا إلى تعلمه. والمعارضة المذكورة وإن فرضنا إتمامها لا تدل إلا على الاستغناء عن تعلم المنطق، وهو لا يناقض الاحتياج إليه، فلا يبعد أن لا يحتاج إلى تعلم المنطق لكونه ضرورياً بجميع أجزائه أو لكونه معلوماً بشيء آخر، وتكون الحاجة ماسة إليه نفسه في تحصيل العلوم النظرية؛ فالمذكور في معرض المعارضة لا يصلح للمعارضة، لأنها المقابلة.
فإن قلت: إذا كانت هذه المباحث بديهية فلا حاجة إلى تدوينها في الكتب. قلت: في تدوينها في الكتب فائدتان: إحداهما إزالة ما عسى أن يكون في بعضها من خفاء محوج إلى التنبيه، وثانيتهما أن يتوصل بها إلى المباحث الأخرى الكسبية.
فإن قيل: استفادة البعض الكسبي من البعض البديهي إنما تكون بطريق النظر، فيحتاج في معرفة ذلك النظر إلى قانون آخر فيعود المحذور. قلنا: ذلك النظر أيضاً بديهي، فالكسبي من المنطق مستفاد من البديهي منه بطريق بديهي، فلا حاجة إلى قانون آخر أصلاً.
قيل عليه: إنما يلزم ذلك إذا قرر كلام المعارض على ما وجهه به، ولنا أن نقرره هكذا: لو كان المنطق محتاجاً إليه، لكان إما بديهياً أو كسبياً، وكلاهما باطل: أما الأول فلأنه يلزم الاستغناء عن تعلمه - وليس كذلك - وأما الثاني فللزوم الدور أو التسلسل في تحصيله. وعلى هذا فقد دلت المعارضة على نفي الاحتياج إلى المنطق نفسه، وحينئذ يجاب بذلك الجواب.
ورد: بأن إبطال كونه بديهياً أو كسبياً يدل على انتفائه في نفسه، ولا تعلق له بكونه محتاجاً إليه أو غير محتاج إليه، إذ يصح أن يقال: ليس المنطق شيئاً لا يحتاج إليه - وإلا لكان إما بديهياً أو كسبياً - وكلاهما باطل - فوجب أن يكون محتاجاً إليه. فظهر أن هذه شبهة يتمسك بها في نفي هذا العلم سواء احتيج إليه أو لم يحتج.
ولنا أيضا أن نقول في تقرير المعارضة: المنطق كسبي، فلا يحتاج إليه في اكتساب النظريات المحتاجة إلى المنطق: أما الأول فلأنه لو لم يكن كسبياً لكان بديهياً - وهو باطل، وإلا لاستغني عن تعلمه - وأما الثاني فلأنه لو احتيج إليه مع كونه كسبياً لزم الدور أو التسلسل. ولم يلتفت الشارح إلى هذا التقرير إذ كان المناسب حينئذ أن يقدم المصنف ذكر النظري وأن يشير إلى لزوم الدور أو التسلسل في اكتساب النظريات المحتاجة إلى المنطق، لا أن يقتصر على لزومهما في تحصيله في نفسه.
ويمكن أن يقال: لما بين المصنف الاحتياج إلى المنطق نفسه أراد أن يبين أن حاله ماذا: هل هو بديهي بجميع أجزائه حتى يستغنى عن تدوينه في الكتب، أو هو كسبي بجميع أجزائه حتى يمتنع تحصيله - فضلاً عن تدوينه – وبين فساد القسمين، فظهر أن المنطق ليس مما يستغنى عن تدوينه ولا مما يمتنع تحصيله وتدوينه مع كونه محتاجاً إليه، فوجب أن يدون في الكتب. ولم يلتفت الشارح أيضا إلى هذا التوجيه لأن المشهور في كتب الفن إيراد المعارضة في هذا الموضع لنفي الاحتياج إليه.
يعني أن المعارضة مقابلة الدليل بدليل آخر ممانع للأول في ثبوت مقتضاه، وما ذكرتم ليس كذلك.
Know that there are two stations: first, the need for logic itself; second, the need to learn it. The proof only establishes the need for it, not the need to learn it. The mentioned objection, even if we assume it is complete, only indicates independence from learning logic, and this does not contradict the need for it. So it is not unlikely that one does not need to learn logic because it is necessary in all its parts or because it is known through something else, while the need for it itself is urgent in acquiring theoretical sciences. Therefore what is mentioned in the context of objection is not suitable as an objection, because objection is a counter-argument.
If you say: If these topics are self-evident, there is no need to compile them in books. I say: Compiling them in books has two benefits: one is to remove any hiddenness that may require attention, and the second is that through them one may reach other acquired topics.
If it is said: Deriving some acquired parts from some self-evident parts is only by way of reflection, so knowing that reflection requires another law, returning the problem. We say: That reflection is also self-evident, so the acquired part of logic is derived from its self-evident part by a self-evident way, so there is no need for another law at all.
It was objected to him: That follows only if the opponent's speech is formulated as he formulated it. We can formulate it thus: If logic were needed, it would be either self-evident or acquired, and both are false. As for the first, because it would imply independence from learning it - and that is not so. As for the second, because of the occurrence of circularity or infinite regress in acquiring it. And on this basis, the objection indicates the negation of the need for logic itself, and then it is answered with that answer.
And it was replied: That invalidating its being self-evident or acquired indicates its negation in itself, and it has no connection to its being needed or not needed, because it is valid to say: 'Logic is not something that is not needed - otherwise it would be either self-evident or acquired - and both are false - so it must be needed.' Thus it becomes clear that this is a sophism used to negate this science whether it is needed or not.
We can also say in formulating the objection: Logic is acquired, so it is not needed in acquiring the theoretical matters that need logic. As for the first, because if it were not acquired it would be self-evident - which is false, otherwise one would be independent of learning it. As for the second, because if it were needed while being acquired, circularity or infinite regress would occur. And the commentator did not pay attention to this formulation, for the appropriate thing then would be for the author to mention the theoretical and to indicate the occurrence of circularity or infinite regress in acquiring the theoretical matters that need logic, not to restrict to their occurrence in acquiring it itself.
It can be said: When the author established the need for logic itself, he wanted to explain what its state is: Is it self-evident in all its parts so that one can do without compiling it in books, or is it acquired in all its parts so that acquiring it is impossible - let alone compiling it - and he showed the falsity of both parts, so it became clear that logic is not something that can be dispensed with by compiling it nor impossible to acquire and compile while being needed, so it must be compiled in books. And the commentator also did not pay attention to this orientation, because the common practice in the books of the art is to present the objection in this place to negate the need for it.
It means that the objection is a counter-argument with another argument that prevents the first in establishing its consequence, and what you mentioned is not like that.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This is a dialectical passage defending the need for logic against a series of objections. The author (the commentator on Qutbi) begins by distinguishing two stations: the need for logic itself and the need to learn it. The proof (dalil) only establishes the need for logic itself, not the need to learn it. An objection that logic is self-evident and thus does not need to be learned is then addressed. Even if true, this only shows independence from learning, not that logic itself is unnecessary. The author then answers an objection: if the topics of logic are self-evident, why compile them? He gives two benefits: removing hiddenness and enabling acquisition of other acquired topics. A further objection is raised: deriving acquired parts from self-evident parts requires reflection, which would need another law, leading to circularity. The author responds that reflection itself is self-evident, so no separate law is needed. The opponent then reformulates the objection as a dilemma: if logic is needed, it must be either self-evident or acquired; both are false; therefore logic is not needed. The author refutes this by showing that the dilemma actually proves the need for logic, because if logic were neither self-evident nor acquired, it would be something not needed, but that is absurd. Another formulation is proposed: logic is acquired, so it cannot be needed for acquiring theoretical matters (otherwise circularity). The commentator notes that the author (the original matn author) may have had a different intention: to show that logic is neither entirely self-evident (so it needs compilation) nor entirely acquired (so it is impossible), but rather it is needed and can be compiled. The passage ends by clarifying that a valid objection (mu'arada) must counter the proof's conclusion directly, not merely address peripheral issues. Throughout, the key distinction is between need for logic itself and need to learn it, and between self-evident and acquired knowledge.

تَسَلْسُل
بَدِيهِيّ
كَسْبِيّ

A) That logic is not needed
B) That logic must be needed
C) That logic is self-evident
D) That logic is acquired

Key Takeaway
The opponent's dilemma that logic is either self-evident or acquired and both false can be inverted to prove that logic is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your opponent in a theological debate claims: 'If we need to acquire knowledge of the divine attributes, it must be either self-evident or acquired. Both are false; therefore it is impossible.' Using the inversion technique from this lesson, refute the dilemma and show that knowledge of the divine attributes is needed.

The Subject Matter of Logic
Author defines the subject matter of logic as conceptual and judgmental cognitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of logic and explain why it is defined as conceptual and judgmental cognitions based on the passage.

Core Idea
Logic studies mental cognitions, not external objects.

In this passage, the author defines the subject matter of logic, building on a general principle: every science investigates the essential accidents of a specific topic. For logic, that topic is conceptual and judgmental cognitions—the building blocks of all reasoning. As we work through the text, you will see how the logician examines these cognitions from two perspectives: as tools to reach unknown knowledge and as foundational elements that those tools depend on.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general definition of a science's subject matter
Find the sentence starting with 'موضوع كلّ علم'. It states that the subject matter of every science is that which it studies for its accidents—either essential, co-extensive, or partial.
This is a foundational principle for defining any Islamic science.
2
Apply the definition to logic
The text then says 'فموضوع المنطق المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية'. So logic's subject is conceptual and judgmental cognitions.
3
Understand the two aspects of investigation
The logician studies these cognitions from two angles: (1) insofar as they lead to an unknown concept or judgment (i.e., as instruments of reasoning), and (2) insofar as they are the foundational elements on which those instruments depend.
These two aspects are the 'accidents' of the subject matter.
4
List the properties studied for each type
For concepts, the properties include universality, particularity, essentiality, accidentally, genus, species, etc. For judgments, they include being a proposition, its converse, its contrapositive, and more remote elements like subject and predicate.
These are the 'awāriḍ dhātiyyah' of logic's subject.
5
Examine the commentator's expansion on distinguishing a science
The commentator (author of the sharh) explains that a science is not fully distinguished except by knowing its subject matter. He then addresses an objection: that knowledge of a specific subject requires prior knowledge of the general, but he clarifies that only a general belief is needed, not essential knowledge.
This resolves a potential circularity in defining a science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني في موضوع المنطق: موضوع كلّ علم ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه التي تلحقه لما هوهو – أي لذاته أولما يساويه أو لجزئه فموضوع المنطق المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لأن المنطقي يبحث عنها من حيث أنها توصل إلى مجهول تصوّري أو تصديقي، ومن حيث أنها يتوقف عليها الموصل إلى التصوّر - ككونها كلية وجزئية وذاتية وعرضية، وجنساً وفصلاً، وعرَضاً وخاصة – ومن حيث أنها يتوقف عليها الموصل إلى التصديق، إمّا توقفا قريباً - ككونها قضية وعكس قضية ونقيض قضيّة - وإما توقفا بعيداً ككونها موضوعات ومحمولات. اقول : قد سمعت أن العلم لا يتميز عند العقل الأبعد العلم بموضوعه) ولما كان موضوع المنطق أخص من مطلق الموضوع، والعلم بالخاص (١) أي لا يتميز عند العقل مميزاً تاماً ولا تحصل له زيادة بصيرة في الشروع في العلم إلا بعد العلم بأن موضوعه ماذا ; أعني التصديق بأنّ الشيء الفلاني مثلاً موضوع لهذا العلم كما أشرنا إليه سابقاً (شريف). (۲) هذا كلام القوم ويتبادر منه إلى الفهم أن المقصود تصوّر الموضوع اعترض عليه بأن العلم بالخاص مسبوق بالعلم بالعام إذا اجتمع هناك شيئان : أحدهما أن يكون العلم بالخاص علماً به بالكنه، وثانيهما أن يكون العام للخاص، وكلاهما ممنوع في صورة النزاع. ذاتياً
The Second Inquiry: On the Subject Matter of Logic. The subject matter of every science is that in which it investigates the accidents that attach to it insofar as it is what it is – that is, for its essence, or for what is equal to it, or for a part of it. So the subject matter of logic is conceptual and judgmental cognitions, because the logician investigates them insofar as they lead to an unknown concept or judgment, and insofar as that which leads to the concept depends on them – such as their being universal/particular, essential/accidental, genus, species, accident, property – and insofar as that which leads to judgment depends on them, either a proximate dependence – such as being a proposition, converse of a proposition, contrapositive of a proposition – or a remote dependence – such as being subjects and predicates. I say: You have heard that a science is not distinguished by the intellect except after knowledge of its subject matter. And since the subject matter of logic is more specific than absolute subject matter, and knowledge of the specific (1) i.e., it is not distinguished by the intellect with a complete distinguisher, nor does it acquire increased insight in embarking upon the science except after knowing what its subject matter is; I mean the belief that such-and-such thing is the subject of this science, as we indicated previously (Sharif). (2) This is the statement of the people, and it is understood from it that the intended [thing] is the conception of the subject. It was objected that knowledge of the specific is preceded by knowledge of the general if two things are present: one is that knowledge of the specific is knowledge of it by essence, and the other is that the general is predicated of the specific, and both are prevented in the situation of dispute. Essential.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the subject matter of logic. The author first gives a general definition: the subject of any science is that about which it studies its essential accidents (ʿawāriḍ dhātiyyah). Then he specifies that for logic, the subject is conceptual and judgmental cognitions (al-maʿlūmāt al-taṣawwuriyyah wa al-taṣdīqiyyah). The logician examines these cognitions in two respects: first, as they lead to an unknown concept or judgment (i.e., as instruments of reasoning), and second, as they are the foundational elements upon which the reasoning instruments depend. For concepts, this includes properties like universality, particularity, essentiality, accidentally, genus, species, etc. For judgments, it includes being a proposition, its converse, its contrapositive, and the more remote elements like subjects and predicates. The commentator then adds that a science is not fully distinguished except by knowing its subject matter. Since logic's subject is more specific than the general notion of subject matter, one must first know that specific subject. He mentions a common objection: that knowledge of the specific presupposes knowledge of the general, but this is not a problem here because the required knowledge is merely a general belief that something is the subject, not a detailed essential knowledge.

Key Terms
موضوع
المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية
عوارض
قضية

C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of logic is not external reality but the mental cognitions that serve as instruments for attaining knowledge.

What Is Mantiq?

The Subject of a Science and the Subject of Logic
Defines the subject of a science and logic's subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the subject of a science and identify the subject of logic as conceptual and propositional knowledge, using examples from medicine and grammar.

Core Idea
The subject of logic is conceptual and propositional knowledge studied for its essential accidents.

Every science has a subject matter that defines what it studies. In logic, the subject is not just terms and propositions but the conceptual and propositional knowledge that leads from the known to the unknown. This lesson explains what a subject of a science is and what makes logic's subject unique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the subject of a science
Find the Arabic phrase that defines mawdu' al-ilm: 'ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه التي تلحقه لما هو هو' (that in which it investigates its accidents that attach to it insofar as it is itself). Note that it includes essential accidents.
2
Examine the examples of subjects
The human body is the subject of medicine; the word is the subject of grammar. What accidents are studied? Health/illness for body; declension/construction for word.
These examples show how a science's subject is defined by what it investigates.
3
Understand 'essential accidents' (al-awariḍ al-dhatiyya)
They are accidents that attach to a thing for its essence, not from outside. The text says: 'التي تلحق الشيء لما هو هو' (which attach to the thing insofar as it is itself).
Essential accidents are distinct from accidental accidents that come from external causes.
4
Apply the definition to logic
The subject of logic is conceptual and propositional knowledge. Why? Because the logician studies them insofar as they lead to unknown concepts or assents, and studies the means (universal/particular, genus/species, etc.)
This makes logic a tool science (ala) rather than a content science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني في موضوع المنطق: موضوع كلّ علم ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه التي تلحقه لما هوهو – أي لذاته أولما يساويه أو لجزئه فموضوع المنطق المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لأن المنطقي يبحث عنها من حيث أنها توصل إلى مجهول تصوّري أو تصديقي، ومن حيث أنها يتوقف عليها الموصل إلى التصوّر - ككونها كلية وجزئية وذاتية وعرضية، وجنساً وفصلاً، وعرَضاً وخاصة – ومن حيث أنها يتوقف عليها الموصل إلى التصديق، إمّا توقفا قريباً - ككونها قضية وعكس قضية ونقيض قضيّة - وإما توقفاً بعيداً ككونها موضوعات ومحمولات.
أقول : قد سمعت أن العلم لا يتميز عند العقل إلا بعد العلم بموضوعه، ولما كان موضوع المنطق أخص من مطلق الموضوع، والعلم بالخاص مسبوق بالعام وجب أولاً تعريف مطلق موضوع العلم حتى تحصل معرفة موضوع علم المنطق.
فموضوع كلّ علم ما يبحث في ذلك العلم عن عوارضه الذاتية، كبدن الإنسان لعلم الطب - فإنّه يبحث فيه عن أحواله من حيث الصحة والمرض - وكالكلمة لعلم النحو - فإنّه يبحث فيه عن أحوالها من حيث الإعراب والبناء.
والعوارض الذاتية هي التي تلحق الشيء لما هو هو ، أي لذاته.
وأجيب عن ذلك بأن الخاص هاهنا أعني موضوع المنطق مقيد، والعام أعني موضوع العلم مطلق، ولا يتصوّر معرفة المقيد إلا بعد معرفة المطلق وانضمامه إلى ما قيد به. ورد هذا الجواب بأن المطلوب هاهنا ليس تصوّر مفهوم موضوع المنطق حتى يصح توقفه على معرفة مفهوم الموضوع، بل المطلوب معرفة ما صدق عليه مفهوم موضوع المنطق - كالمعلومات التصورية والتصديقية - وليس ذلك مقيدا، فسقط ماذكرتم؛ بل الحق أنه لما كان المقصود التصديق بأن الشيء الفلاني موضوع للمنطق، وذلك لا يمكن إلا بعد معرفة مفهوم الموضوع، لأنه وقع محمولاً في هذا التصديق، فسره أولا. والحاصل أن المطلوب في هذا المقام لو كان تصور ما صدق عليه مفهوم موضوع المنطق لم يحتج إلى معرفة مفهوم الموضوع أصلاً، لأنه عارض له - لا ذاتي له - وأما إذا كان المطلوب التصديق بالموضوعية احتيج إلى بيان مفهومه، سواء جعل في التصديق موضوعاً وقيل : «موضوع المنطق هو هذا» أو جعل محمولاً وقيل : هذا موضوع المنطق
The second investigation: On the subject of logic: The subject of every science is that in which it investigates its accidents that attach to it insofar as it is itself – i.e., for its essence, or for what equals it, or for its part. So the subject of logic is conceptual and propositional information, because the logician investigates them insofar as they lead to a conceptual or propositional unknown, and insofar as the means to conceptualization depend on them – such as being universal and particular, essential and accidental, genus and species, accident and property – and insofar as the means to assent depend on them, either a proximate dependence – such as being a proposition, the converse of a proposition, and the contradictory of a proposition – or a remote dependence, such as being subjects and predicates.
I say: You have heard that a science is distinguished in the mind only after knowing its subject. Since the subject of logic is more specific than absolute subject, and knowledge of the specific is preceded by knowledge of the universal, it is necessary first to define absolute subject of science so that knowledge of the subject of logic is obtained.
So the subject of every science is that in that science investigates its essential accidents, like the human body for the science of medicine – for it investigates its states in terms of health and illness – and like the word for the science of grammar – for it investigates its states in terms of declension and construction.
And the essential accidents are those that attach to a thing insofar as it is itself, i.e., for its essence.
And it was answered to that that the specific here, namely the subject of logic, is qualified, and the universal, namely the subject of science, is absolute, and the knowledge of the qualified cannot be conceived except after knowing the absolute and joining it to what qualifies it. And this answer was refuted by saying that what is required here is not the conceptualization of the concept of the subject of logic so that its dependence on knowing the concept of subject is valid; rather, what is required is knowing what the concept of subject of logic applies to – such as conceptual and propositional information – and that is not qualified, so what you mentioned falls away. Rather, the truth is that since the intent is to assent that such-and-such a thing is the subject of logic, and that is impossible except after knowing the concept of subject, because it occurs as a predicate in this assent, it is first explained. The upshot is that if what is required in this context were the conceptualization of what the concept of subject of logic applies to, there would be no need to know the concept of subject at all, because it is an accident to it – not essential to it. But if what is required is the assent to subjecthood, then explaining its concept is needed, whether it is made subject in the judgement and it is said 'the subject of logic is this', or made predicate and it is said 'this is the subject of logic'.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the Shamsiyyah defines the subject of logic and addresses a potential objection. It begins by stating the second investigation: the subject of logic. The author defines the subject of any science as that which is investigated for its accidents that attach to it in itself, either essentially, co-extensively, or as a part. Then he specifies that the subject of logic is conceptual and propositional knowledge (al-ma‘lūmāt al-taṣawwuriyya wa al-taṣdīqiyya), because the logician studies them insofar as they lead to unknown concepts or assents, and also studies the means to these: for conceptualization, things like universality, particularity, essentiality, accidentalness, genus, species, accident, and property; for assent, proximate means like propositions, their converses, and contradictories, and remote means like subjects and predicates.
Then the commentator raises an issue: it is known that a science is distinguished (in the mind) only after knowing its subject. Since logic's subject is more specific than the general notion of subject, and knowledge of the specific is preceded by knowledge of the general, one must first define the absolute subject of a science. So he gives a definition: the subject of every science is what is investigated for its essential accidents. Examples: the human body for medicine (studied for health and illness) and the word for grammar (studied for declension and construction). Essential accidents are those that attach to a thing in itself, i.e., for its essence.
Then an objection and response are given. The objection says that since the specific subject (logic) is qualified, and the general subject is absolute, one cannot know the qualified without knowing the absolute. The response refutes: what is needed here is not the conceptualization of the concept 'subject of logic' but rather knowing what that concept applies to (the actual information), and that is not qualified. So the objection fails. However, the commentator then gives the correct view: if the aim is to assent that something is the subject of logic, then the concept of subject is needed because it is a predicate. The conclusion is that if one only wants to conceptualize what the term applies to, no concept of subject is needed; but if one wants to assent to subjecthood, explaining the concept is necessary.

Objection and Response: Knowing the Subject of Logic
Addresses an objection about knowing the subject of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection about knowing the subject of logic and analyze the response distinguishing conceptualization from assent.

Core Idea
To assent that something is the subject of logic, you need the concept of subject; to merely know the extension, you do not.

An objection arises: to know logic's subject, must we first know the general concept of 'subject'? The text presents a nuanced response that clarifies the difference between conceptualizing the term and assenting that something falls under it. This lesson unpacks that dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states: the specific subject (logic) is qualified (muqayyad), while the general subject is absolute (mutlaq). Knowledge of the qualified cannot be obtained without knowledge of the absolute and its qualification.
This is a logical priority argument.
2
Understand the initial rebuttal
The response refutes: what is required here is not the conceptualization (tasawwur) of the concept 'subject of logic', but knowing what that concept applies to (musaddaq), i.e., the actual information. That is not qualified, so the objection fails.
The distinction between concept and extension is invoked.
3
Recognize the author's correction
The author says the truth is different: if the aim is to assent (tasdiq) that something is the subject of logic, then the concept of subject is needed because it occurs as a predicate in the assent. So both sides are partially correct.
The author reconciles the two positions.
4
Distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq
Conceptualization is grasping a concept; assent is judging a proposition. For mere conceptualization of the extension, no concept of subject is needed. For judgment (assent), the subject concept is required as a predicate.
This distinction is central to Islamic epistemology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني في موضوع المنطق: موضوع كلّ علم ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه التي تلحقه لما هوهو – أي لذاته أولما يساويه أو لجزئه فموضوع المنطق المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لأن المنطقي يبحث عنها من حيث أنها توصل إلى مجهول تصوّري أو تصديقي، ومن حيث أنها يتوقف عليها الموصل إلى التصوّر - ككونها كلية وجزئية وذاتية وعرضية، وجنساً وفصلاً، وعرَضاً وخاصة – ومن حيث أنها يتوقف عليها الموصل إلى التصديق، إمّا توقفا قريباً - ككونها قضية وعكس قضية ونقيض قضيّة - وإما توقفاً بعيداً ككونها موضوعات ومحمولات.
أقول : قد سمعت أن العلم لا يتميز عند العقل إلا بعد العلم بموضوعه، ولما كان موضوع المنطق أخص من مطلق الموضوع، والعلم بالخاص مسبوق بالعام وجب أولاً تعريف مطلق موضوع العلم حتى تحصل معرفة موضوع علم المنطق.
فموضوع كلّ علم ما يبحث في ذلك العلم عن عوارضه الذاتية، كبدن الإنسان لعلم الطب - فإنّه يبحث فيه عن أحواله من حيث الصحة والمرض - وكالكلمة لعلم النحو - فإنّه يبحث فيه عن أحوالها من حيث الإعراب والبناء.
والعوارض الذاتية هي التي تلحق الشيء لما هو هو ، أي لذاته.
وأجيب عن ذلك بأن الخاص هاهنا أعني موضوع المنطق مقيد، والعام أعني موضوع العلم مطلق، ولا يتصوّر معرفة المقيد إلا بعد معرفة المطلق وانضمامه إلى ما قيد به. ورد هذا الجواب بأن المطلوب هاهنا ليس تصوّر مفهوم موضوع المنطق حتى يصح توقفه على معرفة مفهوم الموضوع، بل المطلوب معرفة ما صدق عليه مفهوم موضوع المنطق - كالمعلومات التصورية والتصديقية - وليس ذلك مقيدا، فسقط ماذكرتم؛ بل الحق أنه لما كان المقصود التصديق بأن الشيء الفلاني موضوع للمنطق، وذلك لا يمكن إلا بعد معرفة مفهوم الموضوع، لأنه وقع محمولاً في هذا التصديق، فسره أولا. والحاصل أن المطلوب في هذا المقام لو كان تصور ما صدق عليه مفهوم موضوع المنطق لم يحتج إلى معرفة مفهوم الموضوع أصلاً، لأنه عارض له - لا ذاتي له - وأما إذا كان المطلوب التصديق بالموضوعية احتيج إلى بيان مفهومه، سواء جعل في التصديق موضوعاً وقيل : «موضوع المنطق هو هذا» أو جعل محمولاً وقيل : هذا موضوع المنطق
The second investigation: On the subject of logic: The subject of every science is that in which it investigates its accidents that attach to it insofar as it is itself – i.e., for its essence, or for what equals it, or for its part. So the subject of logic is conceptual and propositional information, because the logician investigates them insofar as they lead to a conceptual or propositional unknown, and insofar as the means to conceptualization depend on them – such as being universal and particular, essential and accidental, genus and species, accident and property – and insofar as the means to assent depend on them, either a proximate dependence – such as being a proposition, the converse of a proposition, and the contradictory of a proposition – or a remote dependence, such as being subjects and predicates.
I say: You have heard that a science is distinguished in the mind only after knowing its subject. Since the subject of logic is more specific than absolute subject, and knowledge of the specific is preceded by knowledge of the universal, it is necessary first to define absolute subject of science so that knowledge of the subject of logic is obtained.
So the subject of every science is that in that science investigates its essential accidents, like the human body for the science of medicine – for it investigates its states in terms of health and illness – and like the word for the science of grammar – for it investigates its states in terms of declension and construction.
And the essential accidents are those that attach to a thing insofar as it is itself, i.e., for its essence.
And it was answered to that that the specific here, namely the subject of logic, is qualified, and the universal, namely the subject of science, is absolute, and the knowledge of the qualified cannot be conceived except after knowing the absolute and joining it to what qualifies it. And this answer was refuted by saying that what is required here is not the conceptualization of the concept of the subject of logic so that its dependence on knowing the concept of subject is valid; rather, what is required is knowing what the concept of subject of logic applies to – such as conceptual and propositional information – and that is not qualified, so what you mentioned falls away. Rather, the truth is that since the intent is to assent that such-and-such a thing is the subject of logic, and that is impossible except after knowing the concept of subject, because it occurs as a predicate in this assent, it is first explained. The upshot is that if what is required in this context were the conceptualization of what the concept of subject of logic applies to, there would be no need to know the concept of subject at all, because it is an accident to it – not essential to it. But if what is required is the assent to subjecthood, then explaining its concept is needed, whether it is made subject in the judgement and it is said 'the subject of logic is this', or made predicate and it is said 'this is the subject of logic'.

Defining the Subject of a Science and Its General Concept
Defines subject of a science and discusses priority of general concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the subject of a science in terms of essential accidents and explain why the general concept must be known before assenting to the subjecthood of logic.

Core Idea
The subject of a science is defined by its essential accidents, and assent to subjecthood requires prior knowledge of the general concept.

In this passage, the author lays down the foundational definition: the subject of every science is that thing whose essential accidents are investigated. He then tackles a critical question: do we need to understand the general concept of 'subject of a science' before we can identify the subject of logic? The ensuing discussion clarifies the difference between mere conceptualization and assent, and shows that for the latter, the general concept is necessary. This lesson will guide you through that definition and the logical steps of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of subject of a science
Locate the sentence: 'موضوع كلّ علم ما يبحث في ذلك العلم عن عوارضه الذاتية'. This definition states that the subject is that whose essential accidents are studied.
2
Analyze the examples
The author gives two examples: the human body for medicine, and the word for grammar. Identify the essential accidents for each: health/illness for the body, inflection/construction for the word.
These examples illustrate how the definition applies in real Islamic sciences.
3
Distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq
The passage discusses two aims: conceptualization (tasawwur) of what 'subject of logic' applies to, and assent (tasdiq) that something is the subject. Notice the difference: tasawwur is grasping the concept, tasdiq is judging that a particular thing fits that concept.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the objection and its refutation.
4
Trace the objection and refutation
An objector says: the specific (subject of logic) is restricted, and knowing the restricted requires knowing the absolute (general subject). The refutation first points out that the aim is not the concept but the referent, which is not restricted. Then the author gives the true answer: if we want to assent that something is the subject (i.e., judge it as predicate), we do need the general concept.
This shows that the need for the general concept depends on the type of knowledge sought (tasawwur vs. tasdiq).
5
Apply the distinction to another science
Consider the science of fiqh. Its subject is the actions of the mukallaf. The essential accidents investigated are the rulings (wajib, haram, etc.). To assent that 'actions of the mukallaf are the subject of fiqh', do you need the general concept of 'subject of a science'? Yes, because 'subject' appears as a predicate.
This reinforces the logical necessity of defining general terms before specific applications.

The subject of logic is preceded by the general; hence it is necessary first to define the absolute subject of a science so that knowledge of the subject of logic is obtained. For the subject of every science is that whose essential accidents are investigated in that science, like the human body for medicine—for in it one investigates its states in terms of health and illness—and like the word for grammar—for in it one investigates its states in terms of inflection and construction. And essential accidents are those that attach to a thing for what it is, i.e. for its essence. And it was answered that the specific here, namely the subject of logic, is restricted, while the general, namely the subject of a science, is absolute, and knowledge of the restricted cannot be conceived except after knowledge of the absolute and its conjunction with what restricts it. Commentary
This passage defines the subject of a science and then addresses a critical objection concerning the subject of logic. The author begins by stating that since the subject of logic is subsumed under the general concept of 'subject of a science', we must first define the absolute subject of a science. The definition given is: 'The subject of every science is that whose essential accidents are investigated in that science.' Essential accidents are then defined as those that attach to a thing for what it is, i.e., for its essence. The refutation clarifies that what is sought here is not the conceptualization of the concept of 'subject of logic' but rather knowledge of what that concept applies to—namely, conceptual and assent-based cognitions. Since these are not restricted, the objection collapses. However, the truth is that if the goal is to assent that a certain thing is the subject of logic, then the concept of 'subject' must be understood because it appears as a predicate in that assent. The summary distinguishes between two possible aims: if the aim is mere conceptualization of the referent, no prior concept of 'subject' is needed; but if the aim is assent to subjecthood, then the concept must be clarified. This is a nuanced epistemological discussion about the priority of general concepts in defining the subject of a science, using the specific case of logic as an example.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
5 min
Defining the Subject of Logic
Establishes the subject of logic with a restriction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of essential accidents to identify the subject of any Islamic science.

Core Idea
Logic's subject is known concepts and assents restricted by their capacity to lead.

In every Islamic science, the subject is defined by what essential accidents are investigated. This lesson shows how logic's subject is identified: informational concepts and assents, but only insofar as they have the property of leading to the unknown. This careful restriction distinguishes logic from other sciences like ontology or epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle for defining a science's subject
The passage states: 'ما يبحث في العلم عن أعراضه الذاتية فهو موضوع ذلك العلم' – what is investigated for its essential accidents is the subject.
2
Apply the principle to logic
The logician investigates the essential accidents of informational concepts and assents, so these become the subject of logic.
3
Notice the restriction
The logician does not investigate all their states, only those related to 'leading to the unknown' (al-īṣāl ilā al-majhūl).
4
Understand the key term 'validity of leading'
The restriction is not actual leading but the validity (capacity) to lead, which preserves the subject's nature and allows the investigation of the leading itself as an accident.
This distinction (ṣiḥḥat al-īṣāl vs. al-īṣāl itself) is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا تمهد هذا فنقول : موضوع المنطق المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لأن المنطقي إنما يبحث عن أعراضها الذاتية، وما يبحث في العلم عن أعراضه الذاتية فهو موضوع ذلك العلم، فتكون المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية موضوع المنطق، وإنما قلنا : «إن المنطقي يبحث عن الأعراض الذاتية للمعلومات التصورية والتصديقية» لأنه يبحث عنها من حيث أنها توصل إلى مجهول تصوّري أو مجهول تصديقي ، كما يبحث (۱) ليس المراد أنها مطلقاً موضوع المنطق، بل هي مقيدة بصحة الإيصال، موضوع له ; وذلك لأن المنطقي لا يبحث عن جميع أحوال المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية مطلقاً، بل عن أحوالها باعتبار صحة إيصالها إلى مجهول; وتلك الأحوال هي الإيصال ومايتوقف عليه الإيصال; وأما أحوال المعلومات لامن هذه الحيثية - أعني صحة الإيصال ككونها موجودة في الذهن أو غير موجودة و كونها مطابقة لماهيات الأشياء في أنفسها أو غير مطابقة لها، إلى غير ذلك من أحوالها - فلا بحث للمنطقي عنها; إذ ليس غرضه متعلقاً بها. فموضوع المنطق مقيد بصحة الإيصال ، لا بنفس الإيصال ، وإلا لم يصح البحث عن نفس الإيصال، لأنه ليس حينئذ من الأعراض الذاتية، بل قيد للموضوع; بل الإيصال وما يتوقف عليه أعراض ذاتية له يبحث عنها في هذا العلم (شريف). (٢) أحوال المعلومات التصورية التي يبحث عنها في المنطق ثلاثة أقسام : أحدها الإيصال إلى مجهول تصوري : إما بالكنه كما في الحد التام، وإما بوجه ما ذاتي أو عرضي كما في الحد الناقص والرسم التام والرسم الناقص; وذلك في باب التعريفات.
When this is established, we say: The subject of logic is informational concepts and assents, because the logician only investigates their essential accidents, and what is investigated in a science regarding its essential accidents is the subject of that science. Thus informational concepts and assents become the subject of logic. We only said, 'The logician investigates the essential accidents of informational concepts and assents,' because he investigates them from the aspect that they lead to an unknown concept or unknown assent, as he investigates (1) It is not meant that they are absolutely the subject of logic; rather they are the subject restricted by the validity of leading. That is because the logician does not investigate all states of informational concepts and assents absolutely, but rather their states in consideration of the validity of their leading to the unknown; and those states are the leading and what depends on it. As for the states of information not from this aspect—i.e., validity of leading, such as whether they exist in the mind or not, whether they correspond to the essences of things in themselves or not, and other such states—the logician does not investigate them because his purpose is not concerned with them. Therefore the subject of logic is restricted by the validity of leading, not by the leading itself; otherwise the investigation of the leading itself would not be valid, because it would not then be among the essential accidents, but rather a restriction of the subject. Rather, the leading and what depends on it are essential accidents of it that are investigated in this science (Sharif). (2) The states of informational concepts that are investigated in logic are three divisions: one of them is the leading to an unknown concept: either by essence as in the complete definition, or by some essential or accidental aspect as in the incomplete definition, complete description, and incomplete description; and that is in the chapter on definitions.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is definitional, establishing the subject matter (mawdu') of logic. The author begins by stating that the subject of logic is informational (mutlaq) concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat), because the logician investigates their essential accidents. Following the principle from classical sciences that the subject of a science is that whose essential accidents are investigated, this makes tasawwurat and tasdiqat the subject. However, the author immediately qualifies: the logician does not investigate all their properties, only those related to 'leading to the unknown' (al-īṣāl ilā majhūl). The footnote (1) clarifies that the subject is restricted by the property of 'validity of leading' (ṣiḥḥat al-īṣāl), not by the actual leading itself. Thus properties like existence in the mind or correspondence to reality are not the concern of logic; only the capacity to lead to the unknown and what depends on it. The footnote (2) elaborates that the states of informational concepts investigated are three: leading to an unknown concept via complete definition, incomplete definition, complete description, or incomplete description—all covered in the chapter on definitions. This careful restriction distinguishes logic from other sciences like ontology or epistemology.

معلومات تصديقية

The Scope of Logic: What Is Studied and What Is Not
Clarifies which properties of concepts and assents are investigated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between properties of concepts that are relevant to logic and those that are not.

Core Idea
Logic investigates only the property of 'validity of leading' and what depends on it, not existence or correspondence.

This lesson focuses on the boundary of logical inquiry. The passage clarifies that the logician does not study every aspect of concepts and judgments, but only those that pertain to their capacity to lead to the unknown. This distinction is crucial for understanding the practical purpose of logic in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the restriction on the subject
The logician only investigates informational concepts and assents insofar as they have the property of 'validity of leading' (ṣiḥḥat al-īṣāl).
2
List the states that are studied
The states studied are: the leading itself (al-īṣāl) and what it depends on (for example, definition, description).
Footnote (2) gives three specific divisions for concepts.
3
List the states that are excluded
Excluded states include: existence in the mind, correspondence to reality, universality, etc. The passage explicitly says 'كونها موجودة في الذهن أو غير موجودة وكونها مطابقة لماهيات الأشياء'.
4
Understand the reason for exclusion
The logician's purpose is not to study existence or truth, but to provide rules for acquiring unknown knowledge from known knowledge. Therefore, only properties that aid this process are investigated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Subject Matter of Logic: Definition and Qualification
Author defines and qualifies the subject matter of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to delineate the precise subject matter of logic according to the Islamic logical tradition, including the qualification of sound conveyance, and distinguish which aspects of cognitions are studied in logic and which are not.

Core Idea
Logic studies only cognitions that can soundly convey to the unknown, not all mental content.

Now that the foundations are laid, the author turns to the crucial task of defining the exact subject matter of logic. This is not merely a formality; it determines the scope and purpose of the entire discipline. The passage carefully specifies that logic deals with conceptual and assent-based cognitions, but only under a specific condition: their capability to correctly lead to unknown knowledge. Without this qualification, logic would be indistinguishable from psychology or epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Find where the author states that the subject matter of logic is conceptual and assent-based cognitions. Note the reasoning given: because the logician investigates their essential accidents, and the subject of a science is that whose essential accidents are studied.
This is the standard 'subject-definition' method in Islamic sciences.
2
Understand the qualification
The author then adds a crucial restriction: the subject matter is not these cognitions absolutely, but only when qualified by 'soundness of conveyance' (ṣiḥḥat al-īṣāl). Locate this qualification and see how it narrows the scope.
The commentary by al-Sharif emphasizes this point.
3
Distinguish what is excluded
The author lists examples of aspects not studied in logic: existence in the mind, conformity to reality, etc. Identify these and understand why they fall outside logic's purview.
This helps avoid confusion with other sciences like psychology.
4
Differentiate subject matter from essential accidents
Recognize that 'conveyance' and 'what depends on it' are presented as essential accidents of the subject matter, not the subject itself. The subject is the cognitions, and the accidents are the properties studied.
This is a key structural distinction in defining any science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا تمهد هذا فنقول : موضوع المنطق المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية ، لأن المنطقي إنما يبحث عن أعراضها الذاتية، وما يبحث في العلم عن أعراضه الذاتية فهو موضوع ذلك العلم، فتكون المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية موضوع المنطق، وإنما قلنا : «إن المنطقي يبحث عن الأعراض الذاتية للمعلومات التصورية والتصديقية» لأنه يبحث عنها من حيث أنها توصل إلى مجهول تصوّري أو مجهول تصديقي ، كما يبحث (۱) ليس المراد أنها مطلقاً موضوع المنطق، بل هي مقيدة بصحة الإيصال، موضوع له ؛ وذلك لأن المنطقي لا يبحث عن جميع أحوال المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية مطلقاً، بل عن أحوالها باعتبار صحة إيصالها إلى مجهول؛ وتلك الأحوال هي الإيصال ومايتوقف عليه الإيصال؛ وأما أحوال المعلومات لامن هذه الحيثية - أعني صحة الإيصال ككونها موجودة في الذهن أو غير موجودة و كونها مطابقة لماهيات الأشياء في أنفسها أو غير مطابقة لها، إلى غير ذلك من أحوالها - فلا بحث للمنطقي عنها؛ إذ ليس غرضه متعلقاً بها. فموضوع المنطق مقيد بصحة الإيصال ، لا بنفس الإيصال ، وإلا لم يصح البحث عن نفس الإيصال، لأنه ليس حينئذ من الأعراض الذاتية، بل قيد للموضوع؛ بل الإيصال وما يتوقف عليه أعراض ذاتية له يبحث عنها في هذا العلم (شريف).
When this is established, we say: The subject matter of logic is conceptual cognitions and assent-based cognitions, because the logician only investigates their essential accidents, and whatever in a science is investigated for its essential accidents is the subject matter of that science. Thus conceptual and assent-based cognitions are the subject matter of logic. We said: 'The logician investigates the essential accidents of conceptual and assent-based cognitions' because he investigates them insofar as they convey to an unknown conceptual or an unknown assent, as he investigates (1) It is not meant that they are absolutely the subject matter of logic, but rather they are qualified by soundness of conveyance as subject matter for it. That is because the logician does not investigate all states of conceptual and assent-based cognitions absolutely, but rather their states with respect to the soundness of their conveyance to the unknown. Those states are conveyance and what conveyance depends on. As for states of cognitions not from this respect—namely soundness of conveyance, such as their being existent in the mind or not, their conformity to the essences of things in themselves or not, and other such states—the logician does not investigate them, for his purpose is not concerned with them. So the subject matter of logic is qualified by soundness of conveyance, not by conveyance itself; otherwise investigation of conveyance itself would not be valid, because then it would not be among the essential accidents but rather a qualification of the subject matter. Rather, conveyance and what depends on it are essential accidents subordinate to it investigated in this science (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter (موضوع) of logic. The author establishes that the subject matter is conceptual cognitions (تصورات) and assent-based cognitions (تصديقات) together. The reasoning is that the logician studies the essential accidents (أعراض ذاتية) of these cognitions, and the subject of a science is that whose essential accidents are studied. He then immediately qualifies this: it is not that the logician studies all aspects of these cognitions. Rather, he only studies them under the aspect of their capability to convey (إيصال) to something unknown, whether conceptual or assent-based. The commentary by al-Sharif explains that the subject matter is further restricted: it is the cognitions considered as qualified by soundness of conveyance (صحة الإيصال), not just any conveyance. For example, whether a cognition is existent or non-existent in the mind, or accurate or inaccurate, is irrelevant to logic. The logician cares only about the property of being soundly conveyable. The conveyance itself (نفس الإيصال) is not the subject matter but a consequence; rather, the subject matter is the cognitions under the condition of sound conveyance. The essential accidents studied are conveyance and its prerequisites. This is a precise delimitation of the scope of logic, ensuring that it is a tool for guiding thought, not a comprehensive study of mental content.

المعلومات التصورية
المعلومات التصديقية
الأعراض الذاتية
صحة الإيصال

Key Takeaway
Logic's subject matter is restricted to conceptual and assent cognitions insofar as they are capable of soundly conveying to the unknown; all other aspects of cognition belong to other sciences.
